The Image of the Blind Probe:
It has no tip like other probes and is not sharp-pointed so as not to injure the artery, and it should be thick and smooth in curvature because if it were thin it would cut the artery with its sharpness; rather it should have a fullness of thickness as we have mentioned.
The Ninety-Ninth Chapter.
On the Median Artery.
This artery is produced in the legs in warm lands such as Hejaz and Arabian countries, and in warm lands that are slightly fertile and perhaps it is produced in other places of the body besides the legs, and it is produced from a swelling that happens beneath the skin.
As it happens inside living creatures, and worms, a swelling occurs in the squash [gourd] and worms that are produced between the skin and flesh, and a sign of the beginning of this artery's occurrence is that in the leg there is severe inflammation then the place erupts and then the artery begins to emerge from the place of that eruption.
As if it were the root of a plant or animal, and if its tip appears then it should be wrapped on a small piece of lead whose weight is from one dirham to two dirhams, and it is tied up and the lead is left hanging from the leg.
So whenever something emerges from it, it is wrapped in lead and tied up, and if it becomes long then some of it is cut and the remaining part is wrapped and do not cut it from its root before all of it emerges because if you cut it, it will shrink and go back into the flesh, causing a boil, a swelling in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it should be managed and pulled little by little until it emerges from its end and nothing of it remains in the body. And this artery may emerge from some people a length of five or ten fingers.
I was told that a man had an abscess which was twenty finger-breadths long, and that while treating him it burst open. So I introduced a reed into its hole and placed on it a strip of cloth long like the body until everything that was in it came out, and I tried to heal the place with medicines for days.
Then I treated him with the treatment of tumors, and this abscess may have many openings, especially if it appears in the joint of the leg or in the leg itself, as it produces many mouths and from each mouth a branch emerges. I treated it as mentioned in the classification and what has come before.
The Twenty-Ninth Chapter.
In the split on the worm that is generated under the knee and it is called a cow's ailment.
This disease is called in some countries among us, cow's ailment, because it often affects cattle. It is a small worm that is generated between the skin and flesh and crawls throughout the body, ascending and descending.
It becomes perceptible to the touch when it crawls from one organ to another until it breaks through where it has broken through the skin, coming out and forming it from pus of some humors as in worms and live things and the love of gourd in the belly,.
And it is expected from its harm that if it crawls in the body and ascends to the head and reaches the eye, perhaps it will open there and come out, thus blinding the eye, and this often happens. So if you want to treat it and remove it, it can only be done when it crawls and becomes perceptible to the touch.
Then you should tighten what is above and below it with a strong bandage, then make a cut on it and remove it. If it has gone deep into the flesh and you cannot find it, apply heat to the place until it burns. The greatest thing that is expected from its damage is the corruption of the eye, as we have said.
If you find it to have become in the head near the eye, pull firmly under it on the forehead and then cut upon it and bring it out. It is necessary that the patient be treated with purification of his body by medicines that are laxative for the foul humors and avoidance of foods that generate putrefaction.
Chapter Thirty-Nine
On the incision for the ailment known as "the deflagrator."
This illness which is called in our country "the deflagrator" is a pain that appears in some organs, then moves from organ to organ, and I have seen it as I describe it to you.
I was summoned to a sick woman in some wilderness, and she uncovered her arm, and I felt a slight swelling in the armpit of the arm. When an hour had passed, I saw that swelling creeping up towards her shoulder like a worm, as swiftly as quicksilver could.
If it moved from place to place, the pain disappeared from that place and became fixed in the shoulder, then I sat for an hour and it spread throughout her entire body until it reached the other arm, then she told me that it was circulating throughout her entire body as I had witnessed.
I was amazed by the speed of its transition from organ to organ, and I had not before seen this illness with my own eyes in this manner, except that I saw a group finding the pain moving from organ to organ, but I did not see it with my own eyes as I saw it in this woman.
And I did not appreciate it except that because the woman was from the people of the wilderness, with dry body and exposed veins, so from here, the feeling of that circulating air became apparent, and it is necessary that it would not appear on this scale in those who are comfortable and have plump bodies with hidden veins.
So if you want to treat it and the sufferer feels the pain, and if it appears before your eyes as we have mentioned, pull firmly above it and below it with haste, and cut upon it until that trapped air comes out and the place is emptied.
If you do not see it with your eye, treat it by rubbing the body and what expels and spreads air like the seed of  the cypress fruit and the seed of the muskmelon, and similar medicines.
Chapter forty-nine.
On extracting arrows.
Arrows differ according to their types and according to the places they are in the body. As for their difference according to types, some are large and some small, some are hollow glass and some solid glass, and some have three angles and some four angles.
And some have tongues and some have splinters. As for the difference according to the organs they are in, they are of two kinds: either the organs are main hollow organs like the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and similar ones. So when an arrow is in one of these organs and the signs of death, which I describe to you after, appear, then it is necessary that you avoid extracting that arrow from it because death will befall its owner in most cases. And when these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow is not deeply hidden in the organ, then extract it and treat the wound. And from the signs of the brain if an arrow hits it and pierces bone and wounds the membrane on the brain, then it causes severe headache, dizziness, and fainting.
And when these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow is not deeply hidden in the organ, then extract it and treat the wound. And from the signs of the brain if an arrow hits it and pierces bone and wounds the membrane on the brain, then it causes severe headache, dizziness, and fainting. And redness in the eyes and redness of the tongue, stiffness, confusion of mind, vomiting, and perhaps blood comes from the nostrils or ears and perhaps speech stops and the voice disappears and from the site of the wound, a white liquid similar to porridge flows and it runs like meat water.
So if these signs appear to you, refrain from treating the patient and extracting the arrow if it has not come out. And as for the signs of the arrow if it hits the heart and is close to the left breast and he feels as if it is rooted in something solid and not something empty.
Perhaps the arrow's movement resembles the beating of the pulse and black blood flows from the wound, followed by coldness in the limbs, cold sweat, and a feeling of heaviness. Know that death is inevitable. A sign of an arrow piercing the lung is the exit of frothy blood from the wound.
The vessels that follow the neck swell and the color of the patient changes, and he breathes heavily and asks for cold air. If the arrow has reached the diaphragm in the chest, it will be near the lower ribs, and breathing will be great with severe pain.
And he groans and all the organs of the groin move, and if the arrow has hit the liver, there will be severe pain and blood resembling the color of the liver will exit from the wound. If the arrow has hit the stomach, perhaps something undigested will exit from the wound.
And his condition is apparent, and if the arrow has hit the abdomen and it swelled and something of feces or feces or a ruptured intestine came out of the wound, there is no hope for healing or removing the arrow. If the arrow has hit the bladder and urine exits.
And something protruded from it and the pain intensified on the patient, know that he is doomed. As for the other organs such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, lower back, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar organs, they are mostly affected.
When the arrow did not hit an artery or nerve and was not poisoned, and I tell you some of what I have seen from the matter of these arrows so that you may infer from it your treatment, namely, an arrow had struck a man in his eye socket at the root of the nose.
And I removed it for him from the other side under the earlobe and he healed without any harm to his eye, and I removed another arrow from a Jew who had been struck in the fleshy part of his eye below the lower eyelid, and the arrow had burrowed and I did not reach except its small end that sticks to the wood.
A large share of the arrows of composite weaponry was a square iron arrowhead, smooth and without barbs; it was forged by a Jew, and no misfortune befel him with its use. Another arrow was extracted from the throat of a Christian, and it was an Arab arrow.
This one had barbs, and it split between the collarbones; it had entered his throat, so he was stung by it until they extracted it, and the Christian recovered and was cured. Another arrow was extracted from a man who had been struck in the belly, and we estimated that he would die from it.
But when he remained for about thirty days, and nothing changed in his condition, the arrow split and became discolored, so they extracted it and he recovered and no misfortune befell him. I saw a man struck by an arrow in his back, and the wound healed on its own.
But when it was seven years later, the arrow emerged at the root of his hip. And I saw a woman who had been struck in her belly by an arrow, and the wound healed, and the arrow remained, and nothing changed in her condition; she did not feel any harm from it in any of her natural activities.
And I saw another man struck by an arrow in his face, and the wound healed, and he did not feel much pain from it. And there are many like this. I extracted an arrow from a man of the sultan's guard who had been struck in the middle of his nose, and it had slanted slightly to the right.
And the arrow had gone completely missing. I was summoned to treat him after three days of the arrow being lodged, and I found the wound from the arrow very narrow; I explored it with a thin probe, and I did not feel it. He felt a throbbing and pain under his ear from the right incision.
So I thought that the throbbing might be from the side of the arrow, so I covered the place with a poultice that had strength to draw and ripen; hoping that the place would swell and show a sign of the arrow. So I incised it, but nothing happened in the place to indicate that the arrow had reached there.
I continued to apply the dressing to it for many days and nothing happened, so the wound closed during that time and the patient remained unable to remove it until one day he felt a pain in his nose and informed me. So I placed on the wound a pungent and acrid drug for many days.
Until it opened and I probed it, and I felt the fine point of the arrow sticking in the wood, then I increased the opening of the wound with that pungent drug until we saw the tip of the arrow with our own eyes, and it took me with him a period of time of about four months.
Then when the wound widened and I was able to enter the probes into it, I pulled and moved it, but it did not respond to coming out, so I continued to soothe it and maneuver it with various instruments until one day I grasped it with firmly fitted probes as shown in the image at the end of the chapter.
And the physicians used to rule that the cartilage of his nose does not close, but I closed it and the wound healed and the patient recovered completely without any harm whatsoever, and I tell you how to extract some arrows so that this will be a measure and evidence of what I have not mentioned.
Because the parts of this craft and its details are not grasped by description nor encompassed by a book, but rather the skilled craftsman measures by little to much and with what has come upon what is absent and derives new work and new tools when strange cases arise.
If I descend from this craft, I say that arrows are extracted from the organs in which they were lodged in two ways: either by pulling from the place where it entered or from the opposite side, and those that exit from where they entered.
Or if the arrow is prominent in a place of flesh, it is pulled and extracted; but if it does not respond to coming out from the time it entered, then it is necessary for you to leave it for days until the surrounding flesh putrefies so that it is easier to pull and extract it, and likewise if it lodges in bone.
If it does not come out, leave it also for some days and urge it and move it every day. If it does not come out after some days, then it is necessary to pierce around the arrow in the bone itself from each side with a fine hole until it widens for the arrow, then pull it and remove it.
If the arrow is lodged in the skull and it has entered one of the cavities of the brain, and some of those symptoms which I have mentioned to you appear on the patient, then hold back from pulling out the arrow and leave it until his condition clears up after some days.
Then attempt to pull out the arrow and remove it. If it is stuck firmly and does not come out, then use the incisions around the arrow as I have described to you, then treat the area until it heals. But if the arrow has disappeared into a place in the body... ...and is lost to sensation, then search for it with a probe. If you feel it, pull it out with some tools suitable for pulling it. If you are unable to do so because of the narrowness of the wound and the depth of the arrow in the cavity, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein there...
...then make an incision until the wound widens and you can get to the arrow so that you remove it. If it has barbs, grasp them and free the flesh around them from each side with all the skill you can. Hold on if you are unable to free the flesh around the barbs. Cut and separate them until you are free.
And if you attempt to remove the arrow from any place, use your hand wrapped with bandages in all directions until you free it and be extremely gentle so that the arrow does not break, making it difficult for you to pull it out and remove it. If you are unable to do so within a reasonable time...
Leave him for days until the flesh around it decays, and then he will be easier to operate on. If you encounter bleeding, use what was mentioned in its chapter regarding treatment, and be cautious of severing any artery, nerve, or tendon; instead, use cunning.
With all your effort, carefully and gently extract the arrow, making sure it is secure as I described. When pulling out the arrow, you should make the patientâ€™s body resemble its state when the arrow was lodged, as this is more appropriate.
If you cannot achieve this, use whatever methods are available to you.  As for the arrow that exits from the opposite direction, either something may protrude outwards or, by feeling with your hand above the skin, you will find the tip of the arrow close by.
Make an incision, being careful to make it as wide as the shafts of arrows permit. Then pull on it; if it sticks in bone, use your hand to rotate until the shaft presses against the bone and widens a space for itself, then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave him for days and attend to him again until it exits. If the shaft of the arrow remains inside, push it out; if the shaft has fallen out and you wish to use propulsion, insert the grooved instrument into its wound and then push it out using that.
If the arrow is hollow, push it out using an instrument that fits inside it; this makes the arrow easier to remove. If the arrow is poisoned, you should excise all the flesh that has come into contact with the poison if possible, and then treat it accordingly.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, abdomen, bladder, or flank and is close to what can be felt with the probe, and you are able to make an incision on it, make the incision carefully avoiding severance of any artery, nerve, and extract it.
Then suture the wound if it needs it, then treat it until it heals. Picture of the arrowheads that are used to attract arrows:.
Their edges are like the beak of a bird, and they are made as if they were a  cooling tool when it grabs an arrow or anything else, and it does not let go. And they can be made in large, small, and medium types, all according to the size of the arrow, its smallness, the width of the wound, and its narrowness.

The Fifty-Ninth Chapter.
On opening veins.
The veins that are customary to open in the body are thirty, six of them in the head: The two pulsating arteries behind the ears, known as the "hassisins," and the two arteries in the visible temples, and the two veins in the eye sockets, known as the "nazarin."
And the vein standing in the middle of the forehead, and the vein at the tip of the nose, and the two vessels in the neck, and the two veins in the upper lip from the mouth, and the two veins in the lower lip, and these veins are known as "jeharak," and the two veins under the tongue.
As for the veins that are opened in the arm and hand, there are five: One is called "qayfal" which is on the outside side, and it is commonly called the "head vein," and "akhal" which is the middle vein, and it is composed of a branch of "basliq" and a branch from "qayfal," and it is commonly called the "body vein."
And "basliq" which is situated on the inside side, and it is also called "armpit," and it is commonly called the "belly vein," and "hand cord" which is situated on the forearm, and it is the one that sticks to it, and it is the one that appears above the thumb clearly, and "aselem."
This is the vein that lies between the index finger and middle finger, it has two branches, and in the leg and foot there are three veins: one that is under the knee cap from the outside side, the second is the saphenous vein and its location is at the ankle from the inside side, and the vein of the gluteus maximus and its location is at the heel from the outside side.
And in the other leg there are three veins like them, as for the two veins behind the ears, their benefit is to cut them for chronic headaches, migraines, and scalp ulcers and chronic bad scalp wounds, and how to cut them as I describe.
This is by shaving the patient's head and rubbing the back of his head in the location of the two veins with a rough cloth rubbed well, then strangle the patient's neck with a scarf until the two veins appear and their location behind the ears in the lower positions on the head so you resemble them with your finger and where you feel their pulse under your finger there you prick it with the stylus.
Then take a scalpel, which is known as the nashla, and then insert it under the vein in the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone, then raise your hand with the vein and skin upwards and cut the vein with the skin, a clean cut, and the length of the cut should be the length of two fingers clenched together.
Or similarly, you send out the amount of blood you want, then tie them with strips and leave them until they heal. And I have mentioned in the beginning of this book their cutting and cauterization. As for the two arteries in the temples, their benefit is to cut them for chronic migraines, severe headaches, persistent blindness, and acute discharge that flows sharply towards the eyes.
And how to cut them as I describe to you: the patient tightens his neck with a scarf until the veins appear clearly for feeling and their pulse is clear under your finger, then you prick it with the stylus, then raise the skin from above the vein to above with your index finger.
Insert the scalpel from below and raise the vein above, and excise it as you did with the other veins, and send the blood away according to your need, then tie the patient's wound and place your finger on the vein for a while.
Then place cotton and a bandage on it, and tighten tightly above it, and leave it until it heals.  This has been mentioned before with its description, cutting, and treatment in the beginning of the book. As for lancing the frontal vein, its benefit is after lancing the temple vein for chronic ailments of the face like eczema, sores, and redness.
And how to lance it as I describe to you, strangle the patient's neck with a turban until the vein appears, then take the instrument called the axe, and this is its image:
Place the spike at the head of the axe on the same vein and strike from above with a comb or something similar to it, and leave the blood to flow to the extent you desire, then tie the patient's wound and tighten it until it heals.
And it may be opened with a wide scalpel, except that the scalpel should not be sharp-pointed like other scalpels but rather slightly blunt at the end and lanced on the rotation because the bone is close by, and perhaps the scalpel will break if it is thin.
As for the veins in the hollows of the eyes, their benefit is for ailments of the eye from scabies, redness, and swelling, and diseases of the face. As for how to lance them, it is that the patient's neck be tightened with a turban, then lance them while you are standing on his head, and let the lancing be on a rotation towards length a little with a small scalpel slightly wide.
For the location has no flesh in it, so if the scalpel is thin-pointed, it might break, then send away the blood to your need and place cotton on them and tighten them for one night, then tie them, and as for lancing the nasal vein, its benefit is from acute fever.
Severe headache and diseases of the face, such as red runny nose, especially if it is chronic, and how to treat it: the patient should hold his neck tightly, then you take his nose with your left hand and take a thin, long scalpel.
And insert it in the middle of the nostril itself between the partition of the nose straight, because the sweat does not appear to the touch there, so the blood will come out from its time, and you should concentrate your hand on the scalpel a little and send from the blood what you need, then tie it for the night, because it will be cured quickly.
As for the  tonsils, their benefit in treating shortness of breath and the beginning of leprosy and black diseases that appear on the surface of the skin such as black leprosy, scabies, bad ulcers, and sores, and how to treat them: the patient should hold his neck tightly under them with a rope and the craftsman stands on the head of the patient.
And the patient is sitting on a chair, then you cut the sweat to its length with a wide cut slightly, then you dig out from the blood the moderate amount or according to what you see from the need for it, and then you do the same with the other sweat, then you tie the rope.
And tighten the two sweats moderately so that the patient does not choke and leave him until tomorrow because the wound will heal, as for the  veins of the cheek, their benefit in treating them after cutting the jugular vein is that it benefits from sores in the mouth, gum rot, bad ulcers, cracks in the lips, and bad sores.
Those that are in the nose and around it, and how to treat them: you make the patient sit in front of you, tighten his neck with a turban, then turn his lips and look at the two veins that you see one to the right of the lip and the other to its left, and determine them by their blackness.
Also, around these there are slender black veins, so cut them in a clean manner. If it is difficult for you and you do not know what they are, aim to cut the largest and most obvious one. Do the same with the two veins in the lower lip.
As for the two veins under the tongue, their benefit is that they are severed after the cauterization of the nodules in the throat from diseases of the mouth and other ailments.  The way to sever them is: to have the patient sit facing you with his feet pointing towards the sun and raise his tongue and look under his tongue on one side for a vein, and on the other side for another vein, and their color is toward black.
So sever them and take precaution not to make a deep cut, for below them there are arteries, and perhaps blood will flow from those arteries.  As for the three veins that are severed in the elbow, these are the ones that it has become customary to sever for all people, and their severance is done in two ways.
Either by inserting a broad or olive-shaped lance into the vein, or by making an incision with a sharp scalpel.  This is called a lancet.  [And this is its image]. Image of the broad lance.
It should be broad as you see, so it is suitable for opening the dilated veins that are full of thick blood.  And this is the image of the olive-shaped scalpel.
And this scalpel is narrower and with a thinner tip, so it is suitable for severing the slender veins that contain thin, yellowish blood.  And this is the image of the lancet.
This lancet which is suitable for incision and comes in different types, broad or slender, according to the width and narrowness of the veins.  And this can be inferred from it, as it is well-known among craftsmen.
As for the basilica vein, which is one of these three veins, its benefit from being severed is that it draws blood from illnesses that are below the throat and neck to follow the chest and abdomen. And when severing it, he should take precaution and be on the vein's back, for there is an artery beneath it, and if he makes a mistake and adds more to the incision of the scalpel, he cuts that artery.
If bleeding occurs, it is necessary that the incision be not made with a knife but rather an opening by means of a scalpel.  If the bleeding point does not appear clearly, it is necessary to avoid it and go elsewhere or request some of its branches.
Or cut in the place of the arm's cord, for it is clear and you cut it with a scalpel as we have said.  If you want to cut it directly, it is necessary before pulling the arm, to feel the place until you know the pulse location and then mark it with lead.
Then tie the arm and cut the artery with a deflected incision using the scalpel as we have said, taking care that the blow is far from the location of the artery. When you see, upon tightening the bandage, swelling in the place you marked with lead, then that swelling is the inflation of the artery so avoid it.
If you see the blood when bleeding spurt like urine from a boy, and the blood is thin red, know that it is from the artery. Then immediately place your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger. If the bleeding stops, it often stops, so tighten the arm.
Leave it and warn the patient not to neglect it, let him be on his back and do not move it for days until he heals. If the bleeding does not stop and you are overcome, and medicine is not readily available to you in that moment, amputate the artery if it appears to you, as its ends contract and the blood stops.
Or take a walnut shell, split it, and take half of it, and tie it tightly on the site of the artery with a bandage and splints until the next day. If the bleeding stops, otherwise treat it with what was previously mentioned from placing sharp instruments to stop bleeding, and cutting off blood is not difficult in most cases.
For the place of small wounds and the bandage in the arm is known, as for the internal artery, its benefit from cutting it that it draws blood from the top of the head and the bottom of the body to its location because it is made up of a branch of the carotid and a branch of the cephalic artery as we have said.
It is necessary for the incision to be on a neck of the wound, because if there is a nerve beneath it and the scalpel goes deep and hits the nerve, numbness occurs which makes seeing difficult, and perhaps it may not heal at all. This nerve often appears to the touch.
But if it is hidden in some people and is delicate and cannot be seen, then it is necessary to make your incision a notch with the needle and avoid the nerve as much as you can. If the vein is between two nerves, cut the vein lengthwise, but as for the cephalic vein, the benefit of incising it is that it draws blood from the head.
It is beneficial for eye diseases and in this vein especially if you want to cut it, use the olive scalpel or the broad Riehani scalpel because it is the safest of all veins because there is no artery or nerve beneath it.
Except that when you cut, you should avoid the scalp of the muscle with the scalpel and aim for the soft area. It does not harm if it is not hit by the first blow to be re-cut, but perhaps it will swell in some people if it is not cut on the first blow.
But this swelling does not harm it at all, and as for the method of cutting and its effects, and what should be done to repair it, first of all, you should know that cutting is either used only to preserve health.
And as for that which is used in diseases, and what is used to preserve health and its continuity and prevent the occurrence of diseases, it should be a vein from one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the cephalic, the axillary, and the basilic, and the cutting should be at the beginning of spring when signs of fullness appear.
And it should be on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours have passed from sunrise, and as for the cutting which is used in diseases, there is no limited time for it but whenever need and necessity call for it from night or day.
In every hour and in every time, it is not fitting to bleed boys until they reach fourteen years of age, and do not bleed the elderly who have passed sixty years of age. And if someone intends to bleed for any reason whatsoever...
It is fitting to cleanse the intestines before bleeding with a gentle enema if there is much accumulated fecal matter lest it be drawn into the veins during the bleeding from the intestines, excess foulness that harms the main organs. And do not bleed the intoxicated person nor the drunkard nor the drugged person until that state leaves them.
And let him beware of bleeding also after a bout of diarrhea and vomiting, weakness, excessive intercourse, fatigue, exertion, wakefulness, fasting and all that weakens the body or the soul, then look at the thinning of the humors before that if the blood is thick.
With foods and drinks and medicines if he can do that, then he enters the bath if it does not prevent him or he practices some exercise so that the blood is circulated and make his bleeding in the middle of the day as we said, and he aims to empty his chest that day from all bad psychic ailments.
Such as worries and anger and fear, and all bodily ailments such as fatigue and excessive exertion and intercourse and the like, and prepare his meeting with things that have become customary for people to use from kinds of perfume and aromas and amusements and the like, every person according to what he can afford.
Then he sits the patient on a cushion that is higher than the cushion on which the person being bled sits, then he extends his arm and the surgeon rubs it with his hand two or three times, then he tightens the bandage with the tourniquet and winds it two or three times and let the tightening be moderate.
Because tightening when it is not moderate, either by excess in tightening prevents the flow of blood or if it is towards relaxation also prevents the flow of blood, then after tightening, it is fitting that he rubs the patient's hands all together with some each other.
Until the veins swell and become apparent to the touch, then the surgeon wipes the incisions with a little of old oil, particularly, and places his index finger of the left hand on the same vein under the place he wishes to sever slightly so that the vein may not retreat and avoid the blow. Because some veins you find like tendons, they retreat when severed, and some are filled with air. So when the surgeon places the lancet on them, they descend under the lancet and deceive the incision; the lancet does not open the vein, and if it opens it, then its opening is narrow.
Therefore, it is necessary that the incisions be firm and that he take his time in all these matters, then bring down the lancet; if it opens the vein from its first pass, otherwise, he repeats the incision slightly below or above that place quickly if the place does not swell.
If the place swells or lacerates, leave it for a day or two and do not tighten the bandage because he may possibly bring on a hot suppuration and he should not enter the bath, then repeat the incision if he wishes; if the lancet went in and the opening was small and the blood ran thin.
And I feared that the amount of blood would not come out that you desire, so repeat the lancet in the same opening; it rests on straightness and increase the opening a little and do this quickly before the place swells because in many people, the place may swell at the small incision.
If you see that it has swollen, do not repeat on it at all because it will not benefit you; put something of thick oil on it because it eases the flow of blood and it is better in this place than the oil itself and from all other ointments as well.
So use thick oil in all your incisions of the veins when the flow of blood is difficult; and the treatment may do this, if it is placed on the place because the blood flows thinly and liquefies if it was thick.
If a large tumor occurs in the site of phlebotomy, it often happens, especially for those who have not had phlebotomy before or whose vein opening was small, then immediately place a sponge soaked in warm water and salt on the tumor and pull it tight for an hour as it will dissolve.
You should do this after the blood has come out of the vein itself or another vein, if anything black or green remains in the place after days, it does not harm, if you wish, then apply something from saffron and bitter extract dissolved together.
Or something from the extract of  the pod and similar, and often a tumor and swelling occurs when bleeding from the spleen so place your hand on it if you find it throbbing when you touch it then that is a bad tumor so beware of putting anything mentioned above on it.
Because it may possibly bleed from an artery but compress it with what is in it to harden the place then treat it with all other treatment until it heals, and it is necessary to withdraw blood for every person according to their strength and what appears of the color that predominates on the blood.
Because if the blood is black, let it exit until it turns red, and likewise if you see it thick, send it until it becomes clear, and also if it is sharp until its sharpness goes away, and it is necessary for someone who is full of strength and needs to remove the blood in one go.
That he should widen the phlebotomy and make the scalpel wide, and for someone who is weak, the opposite of that, and it is necessary to withdraw it in several times and that the hole be narrow, and what is best used in phlebotomy is that it be a curved, heated incision, not a piercing.
And this type of phlebotomy is safe from bleeding and cutting a nerve and it is praised and safest than phlebotomy by width and length, and whoever used to be accustomed when phlebotomizing with the cut, then it is necessary to feed him before the phlebotomy something of bread soaked in pomegranate water or  ginger.
If a person has been cauterized and blood has been discharged from him three or four times, if he is of cold temperament, let him take before phlebotomy bread soaked in the syrup of amber or in honey mixed with saffron or in the syrup of roses.
If giddiness occurs during phlebotomy and its cause is the discharge of a large amount of blood, it is necessary to give him water broth and the thin rosewater syrup, apply a cooling compress with galbanum and rub his chest with it and use all that was mentioned in the section on giddiness.
That which is from vomiting, but as for he who wants to relieve his arm and regulate his blood again, it is necessary for him who was bled excessively and whose strength is weak that the blood be regulated little by little according to strength over consecutive days.
But as for he who wants to relieve his arm and regulate his blood again and whose body is strong, let him do that at seven hours or nine from his first bleeding, but as for he who wants to attract the blood from his body to the opposite side of where it has leaned, it is necessary that he be relieved on the second or third day.
But as for he who in his body has a lot of blood that has become hot and intense and caused fever, it is necessary that blood be discharged from him in one installment and a large amount be taken out and the opening be widened until giddiness occurs after having carefully looked at all the conditions of phlebotomy. And that you place your hand on his pulse when the blood is flowing so that death does not occur instead of giddiness, for this often occurs if the phlebotomist is ignorant and carelessness happens, and it is not necessary if you want to relieve the arm and regulate the blood again.
And if the mouth of the vein has closed and the blood flow is difficult, do not squeeze it hard or twist it with force, for this is very bad, but either leave it until you bleed him again or use a scalpel to remove any clotted blood from the mouth of the vein.
If you place on it something of salt that has dissolved in water, or something of the Faruq elixir or the squinting eye, and you gently press him until blood comes out, if the armpit has swollen, leave it.
And do not touch it until the swelling subsides, and if necessity demands to drain the blood again, then either you cut it above that place or in the other arm or in the other armpit.
As for cutting the arm's tendon, it is cut in place of the kohl and bassil if they are not available or hidden because it is made from them, and how to cut it is that the sick person puts his hand in hot water until the elbow reddens and the armpit appears clearly, then you tie above it with a bandage slightly moderately.
Then you cut the armpit with a slight twist, not horizontally or vertically, and let the opening be wide, and your incision should be above the wrist joint slightly, if blood flow is difficult, return the hand to the vessel with hot water and let the blood flow in the water.
Until it reaches your need, if you are in summer days, you may dispense with returning the hand to hot water and most of what makes blood flow in hot water is in wintertime, and cutting this armpit is safer than all the arteries. Because there is no beating artery or nerve beneath it, and as for cutting the  "asiylam" from the right hand, it is beneficial from diseases of the liver, and how to cut it is that you tighten the wrist with a bandage or your hand after putting it in hot water until the armpit swells.
Because there is no beating artery or nerve beneath it, and as for cutting the  "asiylam" from the right hand, it is beneficial from diseases of the liver, and how to cut it is that you tighten the wrist with a bandage or your hand after putting it in hot water until the armpit swells. And it becomes very clear to the touch then you cut it with a slight twist, and even if you cut off all of it, it does not harm him at all, be careful not to overdo your hand with the scalpel because there is a nerve under the fingers and the place is exposed from flesh, then return the hand to hot water.
Leave it to flow blood into it; for if you do not return it to hot water, the blood will coagulate in the opening of the vein and cease flowing. If you withdraw from the blood the amount needed, put upon the vein oil and salt so that it will not close quickly.
And similarly you should do with every narrow branch, as for the benefit of cutting from the left hand, it is beneficial for spleen ailments, and you should do so in its cutting as you did in the second one, and as for cutting the saphenous vein, its benefit is for diseases that are in the lower body such as illnesses of the uterus.
And retention of menstruation and diseases of the kidneys, ulcers of the thighs and legs that are chronic, and similar diseases; and the way to cut it is to put his leg in hot water and rub him until the veins become full and then tighten above the joint of the leg with a bandage and a vein placed at the heel towards the thumb.
And it branches out in the face of the leg into many branches, so cut it in the widest branch of it or at the heel at its junction, it is better and safer; and if you cut it in the face of the leg, then be cautious from the nerves that are beneath it on the face of the leg.
And make your incision for it by a twisting as if you wanted to amputate it and the scalpel be sharp; if the blood is difficult to come out, then let him put his leg in hot water and leave the blood to flow into it until it empties; if the cutter errs by cutting the vein in the first time.
Then let him return the incision to above slightly, for the place is safe and not fearful of any harm if you are cautious from the nerve as we said, and do so with the saphenous vein from the other leg as well; and as for the sciatic vein, its place is as we said at the heel from the outer side.
And the benefit of cutting it is for pain in the hip if it was from the hot blood, and the way to cut it is to put the sick person in a bath and rub him and tighten his leg from below the hip to above the heel with four fingers by a thin long bandage; because it only appears like that.
If it appears, you must cut it out according to its condition, either by turning it or by cutting it off completely or by making a cut into it, if its place is healthy, and it is in most people very light. If you do not find it...
If it does not appear at all, you should cut some of its strands, which appear on the back of the foot towards the thumb and forefinger, and be cautious of the nerves and send out the amount of blood that you want.
Then loosen the tightness and place cotton on the site of the incision and tighten the area, for it will quickly heal.
The Sixty-Ninth Section.
On cupping and how to use it.
Cupping instruments can be made from horns, wood, copper, and glass, and cupping comes in two types: one is cupping with the condition of drawing blood, and the other is cupping without a condition.
And this cupping that is without a condition comes in two forms.
And the cupping of the shoulders, which are both sides of the neck, and the cupping of the chin, which is under the lower jaw from the mouth, and the cupping of the shoulders, and the cupping of the loins on the tailbone, and the cupping of the elbows, which are in the middle of the arms, and the cupping of the legs, and the cupping of the ankles.
And cupping only draws blood from the fine veins scattered in the flesh, and for this reason, it does not draw out the strength like lancing, and cupping should not be used with fire or without fire in any of the diseases that are due to fullness until the whole body is emptied.
If need demands cupping for disease or by habit, we use it at all times, in the beginning, middle, and end of the month, and whenever else it is required.
For some people, if blood increases in them until it needs to be drawn out by cupping, they feel heaviness and headache in their head. Some feel fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. Some feel itching in their face and side, darkness and swelling in their eyes. And some itch the area where they were cupped.
And some laugh a lot, and some feel the taste of blood in their mouth and have a sore gums and spit up blood. Some sleep more, and some see blood, redness, wounds, and surgeries in their dreams and the like. So whenever we see any of this...
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we order cupping after two or three hours have passed from noon. As for the benefit of cupping the nape (of the neck), it benefits from heaviness in the head and water that flows into the eyes.
But it should be after the whole body has emptied, and this cupping can be a substitute for bleeding the head. It is warned against being used by those who are cold in the head or have a cold, as it harms them greatly.
Therefore, it should not be used by the elderly or those with cold ailments in their head. And whoever gets accustomed to it experiences forgetfulness as a result.
Therefore, it is necessary to order the cupper to lower his hand slightly with the cupping vessel for fear of forgetfulness being generated. As for cupping the armpit, it is a substitute for bleeding the axilla and the groin.
Therefore, it benefits from asthma, shortness of breath, chest tightness, coughing, and fullness. It is necessary to raise cupping the armpit slightly because if it is lowered, it causes weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the temples, it is beneficial for pains that occur in the head, migraines, headaches, and pain in the roots of the teeth. It is a substitute for venesection, and it is appropriate to order the cupper not to insert his hand deeply lest he cut a vein and cause bleeding.
As for cupping under the chin, it is beneficial for sores in the mouth and gum disease and similar diseases of the mouth. It can take the place of venesection of the varicose veins on the lips. As for cupping the shoulders, it is beneficial for palpitations that occur from fullness and heat.
As for cupping the  sides of the abdomen, it is beneficial as it benefits venesection of the three veins:  the basilic, cephalic, and axillary because it draws blood from all those delicate veins in the flesh and draws those delicate veins from other thicker veins until the drawing reaches the three thick veins.
It is necessary to order the cupper not to overdo it in the area because the place is bare of flesh and underneath are nerves and arteries. As for a single cupping applied on the coccyx, it is beneficial for hemorrhoids and sores of the lower part.
It is necessary to order the cupper that the cupping be large and made of brass because the place needs a strong suction, and perhaps a glass cupping would break and require many precautions. As for cupping the legs, it reduces swelling considerably.
Because it draws blood from the whole body and is beneficial for chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder. It brings on menstruation and benefits boils and carbuncles and takes the place of venesection of the saphenous and basilic veins except that it tires the body a lot and causes fainting in most people. Cupping of the ankles has benefits close to those of cupping the legs.
The method of placing the cauteries is that the cautery is placed first empty and it is emptied with a moderate measure and do not prolong the placement of the cauteries but you place them quickly and remove them quickly so that they accept the humors to the place in an even direction and continue repeating this until you see the place has reddened and opened.
And when redness of the blood appeared then you should prescribe and resume the emptying slowly, then look at the condition of the bodies, so whoever was from the people whose flesh is loose with porous pores, it is necessary that he be prescribed one cautery only lest the place be scraped.
And order the surgeon to widen the cautery and deepen it a little and adjust the emptying in gentleness and slight motion, if there is thickening in the blood then it is necessary that he prescribe twice, but in the first time let him open a path for the flow of blood and its water, and as for the second time it is for the purpose of investigating the expulsion of the thick blood.
If the blood was very viscous he repeats the cautery a third time to reach the goal, and in general if we wanted to expel a little blood we are satisfied with one cautery, but if we want to expel much blood we prescribe more cauteries, and if we estimate that the blood is thick.
then it is necessary that we prescribe a deep cautery, and the moderate limit in the cautery is only the depth of the skin, what should not be used from ointments when placing the cauteries and from waters and what the surgeon warns against, but as for whoever whose skin was thick, hard, rough, and with narrow pores.
then it is necessary that the places of the cauteries be anointed with opening, softening, and dissolving ointments, but if it were in summer then like the ointment of Khari'i or the violet ointment or almond butter or pumpkin seed butter.
And as for if it were in winter then like the daffodil ointment or the lily ointment or the chamomile ointment or the hyacinth and its likes, if the phlegm was thick and cold then let the ointment be marjoram butter or nutmeg butter or banana butter or shebsh butter and its likes.
If the cupping area is wide and fleshy, it is best to abstain from oil. These individuals should wash their cupping sites after cupping with rosewater or cold water or grapevine water or pumpkin water or men's water and the like. As for those who have much damp blood...
they should wash their cupping sites with vinegar or water with sumac and the like. As for those who have a thick phlegm, they should wash their cupping sites with old syrup or marjoram water or  a concoction of wormwood or chamomile and the like. It is important to avoid cupping in the bathhouse or after taking a bath.
Rather, it is best to use it after leaving the bathhouse an hour or two later. It is not advisable for anyone to sleep after cupping. It is not advisable for the cupped person and those who are being treated before and after cupping.
It is necessary to first look if the cupped person or those being treated are jaundiced and the predominant thing in their blood is dryness and inflammation, then they should take cooling things like pomegranates, dandelion with vinegar, lettuce, sorrel, lamb's quarters and the like. He should make his food quail, mutton stews,  and hare stew and the like.
And if his temperament is cold, it is best for him to drink honey syrup,  mead or sorrel with a touch of vinegar and consume moderately-aged perfumed wine, which is between the old and the new, he should be ordered to eat little and make his food quail, partridge,  sparrows and dove chicks stews. It is necessary that on the day of cupping and venesection, drink be more than food.
And it may be necessary to give some people, at times,  Faruq elixir or musk medicine or shilajit before cupping and venesection, or after it, to strengthen the main organs and thin the blood. It is not advisable for those who are feverish to drink it.
As for the incisions that are not made with conditions, they are those placed on the liver and spleen and breasts and abdomen and the location of the kidneys and hip because these organs do not tolerate being cut into, but rather one desires to draw blood from one organ to another.
Such as placing an incision on the breasts for a case of boils, or we use it to relieve the organ of cold air that has accumulated in the organ such as placing an incision on the abdomen and navel because it loosens the organ, heats it, and removes the pain to dissolve it.
And it can be placed on the kidneys if there is a blockage or stone in them, by virtue of drawing it out maybe it will open the blockage or remove the stone from its place, and it does the same if placed on the liver and spleen when there is wind that mixes in them, and these incisions may be used empty with suction only.
And they may be used with fire and they may be used filled with lukewarm water in cases of boils, that is to fill the incision, and let it be large with hot water alone or with water that has been cooked with some herbs suitable for this purpose.
Then it is placed filled on the place and held and removed and repeated several times as needed, and this is the image of the incision that is used with fire:.
Its mouth is the size of two open fingers as we have depicted and its depth is half a cubit, and there is a small hole on the side of it near its middle, about the size that a needle can enter, made of Chinese copper or yellow copper with a thick rim.
Smooth, flat and polished so as not to harm the organ when placed upon it, and in its center there is a transverse rod of copper or iron where the candle with fire is placed, and this incision can be made large larger than what we have described and small according to the diseases.
And as for its use, the incisions of boys and thin people are not like the incisions of men and robust bodies, and as for how this incision is placed with fire on the organ, it is to ignite a wick from tightly woven cotton or a small candle from tallow and place it in the center of the rod that is in the middle of the incision.
To make the fire ascend upwards towards below the plate so that it does not burn the body of the patient, then it is placed upon the limb and the finger on the hole mentioned until when we grasp the plate as needed, the finger is removed.
And the steam comes out through that hole and the plate melts on its place, then the wick is advanced in the manner described and it is returned if needed to that, as for the plate used in treating the case of phthisis with water there is no stiff rod nor hole in it.
And it is only used by filling it with water and placing it on the limb, and this is its form:
And this plate, every time it is large enough to contain a lot of water, it is better.
Chapter seventy-seven.
On treating the ingrown nail.
The cauterizing iron is used in most cases in the limbs where plates cannot be placed either because of their smallness like the lip and gums and the like, or because the limb is exposed of flesh like the finger and nose and the like.
And the manner of its use is to take from the cauterizing irons that are in pure fresh water free from impurities, then leave it in fresh water for a day and night until it becomes dry and there is nothing left inside its hollow. Then the body is drained first by venesection or cupping, then the affected limb is wiped.
Until it reddens, then it is placed on it and if it is full it falls off, and if possible the place can be cauterized with the plate, this is more effective for benefit, otherwise wash the place with vinegar then with a lot of water and massage and squeeze it, and if bleeding persists after the falling of the cauterizing iron and it was a discharge, then wet a piece of linen cloth in cold water.
And place it above until the discharge stops, and if the bleeding is excessive, sprinkle powdered alum or saffron or something like that from astringents upon it until the bleeding stops or half a peeled onion is placed on the wound.
Leave it until the cauterizer is stuck in place, for the blood will cease, and if the need arises to rejoin it, do not reattach this one if another can be found. If it refuses to attach, wipe the place with fresh blood.
Or insert a needle into the place until something of the blood comes out, then it is placed. If it feels something of the blood stuck to the place, and if you want to remove it, sprinkle upon it some of the gum arabic or salt or ash, for it will fall from the place.
The Third Gate.
In setting bones.
This gate also is of what is needed in the craft of medicine, which is the setting of fractures and dislocations that occur in bones.
Know, O sons of men, that this gate may be claimed by the ignorant among physicians and the common people, and by those who have never skimmed through a book of the ancients nor read a letter from it.
And because of this, this art has become well-known in our land as a science, and I have not found in it anyone who excels even slightly. And what I have benefited from it, I have benefited from because of the length of my reading of the books of the ancients and my diligence in understanding them until I extracted that knowledge from them, then I adhered to experience and practice throughout my life.
And I have drawn for you in this gate all that encompasses my knowledge. And my experience has followed it after I have brought it closer to you and extracted it from the crowd of prolixity, and summarized it to the utmost summary, and explained it to the utmost clarity, and presented for you in it many pictures of tools that are used in it.

The First Chapter.
There are beautiful and comprehensive matters concerning the matter of fractured bones. It should be presented and before we begin to mention the broken and dislocated limbs one by one, it is necessary to mention in the beginning of this chapter a summary of what must be said and sections. You are compelled first to understand them and stand on their truth, you and whoever is eager to learn this honorable craft besides you.
And I say that when a fracture, dislocation, or sprain or fall happens to someone, it is necessary to hasten first to its separation or easing or both. Unless something prevents it, such as weakness of strength or the one to whom this happened is a child or an old man, regardless of whether he is young or old, or if the time is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then, his diet should be limited to cold legumes, poultry and quail meats, and drinking is prohibited, as well as thick meat, filling with food, and any food that fills the veins with blood until the swelling subsides and there is no expectation of fluid seeping into the site.
Then, the sick person should return to his first way of life as usual. If the broken bone takes its course and starts to heal, then the patient should eat foods that nourish him with a lot of thick and heavy food containing fat like pilaf rice, bread, barley, wheat bran, and beef marrow. And eggs, fatty fish, and thick drinks like that. Because with this regimen, the fracture heals faster and better. Know that if a bone is fractured in strong men or old men, it will never be able to join and fuse back to its original nature.
Because of their dry bones and stiffness, and perhaps what was in the bone at its very softest, like the bones of small children can join together, but nature sprouts on the broken bone from all sides something like glue that thickens, holding it and tightening it until one part adheres to another.
And he binds some to others until it comes in the utmost strength and solidity as it was at first so that nothing hinders it from its actions.
For this reason, it is necessary that the food of the patient should be made up of foods which are firm and thick as we have said, and know that fracture differs in its kinds according to the difference of the limbs because a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head and a fracture of the breastbone is different from a fracture of the vertebra.
And so all the other limbs differ from each other, and I shall come to mention every kind of fracture explained in its chapter separately and distinctly, and a type of bone fracture may also differ within itself.
Because it may be a fracture that is splintered without fragments occurring in it, and it may be a fracture along the length of the bone and there are fragments and projections which are clean and unclean and the fracture may be with a wound and tearing in the skin and the fracture may be a slight crack.
And for each type there is a special technique in its healing as will be mentioned in detail in its place, and what distinguishes a bone fracture is its distortion and bending and protrusion of the feeling and crepitus when you touch it with your hand.
So when there is no apparent distortion in the place, nor crepitus nor feeling when you touch the bone with disorder and the patient does not feel much pain, then there is no fracture but it may be a sprain or a slight fracture or a minor crack, so it is not advisable to move it by stretching and manipulating at all.
But apply to it the medicines that will be mentioned later which are suitable for the place, then bind it gently, and know that if the bone has been splintered and shattered into two without fragments occurring in it except that every part has moved away from its companion.
It is necessary for you to hasten from that time to its setting and making it straight before a hot tumor occurs to it, if a hot tumor occurs to it, then leave it for days until the hot tumor subsides, then set it in any way you can with gentleness and skill.
And know that setting and making it straight is easier than the bone which may have occurred in it splinters, then tighten what will be mentioned later, if the bone has splinters, there is no choice but to extend the broken limb from both sides whether hand or leg.
Whether with your hands if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with the rope and hand, let your placing of the limb be on a level place according to its natural shape until when the broken bone part has extended, then at that time reform those appendages in their places in every way you can with skill and gentleness.
And strive with all your effort that you do not cause pain or suffering to the patient by your actions, and strive that you bring one bone together with its partner in the best form, and it is necessary at that time to feel them and sense them with your hand, if you see anything contrary, then correct it and make it straight to the extent of your ability.
And beware of excessive stretching and strong pulling as many ignoramuses do, and many times they speak of doing this and causing a hot tumor or stagnation in the limb as you have seen from their deeds repeatedly.
Then after the setting, perfection, and tightening of that limb, impose stillness and quiet on it, and warn the patient not to move it during his waking or sleeping time, and upon his turning and upheaval, and at his urination, and all his movements as much as he can, and that he should strive for the limb's position to be a position in which he is secure from pain.
And that when he feels in his position of the limb pain or discomfort, that he transfer it to another position which he does not feel pain with, and strive for that position to be level and straight.
Lest a distortion occur in the member if it is bent, and as for how to tighten the broken limb, I will describe it to you. Know that broken members differ in their smallness and largeness and shapes; what was small among them like the arm, finger, hand, and such.
Where the bandages of linen should be fine and gentle, and what was thick like the thigh, back, and chest, then it is necessary that the bandages be solid because the wide ligament needs to tighten the large limb from every side with an equal pull, so there is no blemish in it.
Then after you have finished smoothing it, you begin to carry the plaster suitable for it. On a flexible mesh over the site of the fracture, then you begin to wind the bandage on the spot of the fracture itself, three or four turns, according to what the limb deserves, and you tighten your hand slightly with the bandage, then you take it to the upper side of the place of the fracture.
With a pull less than your pull on the broken place, then you move away with the wrap from the place of the fracture slightly and relax the tightness little by little until it takes from the correct place something suitable, then you take another bandage and also wind it on the fractured place.
Windings, then you take the wrap to the lower side of the fracture. Let your action in pulling the wrap and relaxing it be as mentioned in the first upper winding, then you place between the bandages of linen or  cloth what is suitable for straightening the curvature of the fracture.
If it has a curvature, otherwise do not put anything in it, then wind another bandage on top of it and smooth the firm plaster splints on these wraps from your time if there is no swelling or hot tumor in the limb.
If it has swelling or a hot tumor, carry on top of it what soothes that tumor and removes the swelling, and leave it for days, then tighten the splints at that time, and let the splints be from half-cut reeds of solid wood expertly prepared, or the splints be from ebony wood.
Those made from cedar or palm leaves or flax and similar materials, whatever is available of that kind, and let the width of each splint be of this shape and size.
Except that the splint placed on the fracture itself should be slightly thicker and wider than the other splints, and as for the length of the splint, it is made according to what suits the broken limb from its size and smallness, image of the splint:
Then you tighten the splints with another bandage according to the tightness of your first one, and then tie it from above with strong threads according to what we mentioned about the tension, which is that the tension on the fracture site should be more. And the closer it is to the fracture, the less tight the bandage should be.
And the threads should be medium in thickness and softness, and they should be of damp cotton, especially because if the threads are thick as you see some ignoramuses do, who make their threads from torn strips of cotton, it is a great mistake.
Because the tension with them goes beyond moderation, and thin threads are also not suitable because you do not achieve what you want with the tension, and there should not be less than a finger between the splint and the splint.
If the patient is injured by the edges of the splints after tightening in the correct places, make under them from soft padding or unstuffed wool so that it does not harm him with anything. As for if the fracture is with a wound and there is a tear in the skin, we will mention it separately.
Know that not every broken limb should be tightened with splints from the first day, for if the member is large, it should not have splints placed on it except after five days or seven or more according to your safety from the occurrence of the hot swelling.
And this is the description of the bandages that were used to bind fractures, dislocations and sprains by those who came before, a description of a general bandage that binds a fracture and is suitable for most temperaments, especially boys and women because it is not overcome by heat or cold.
And it is to take from the flour dust, which is what clings to the walls of the mill when the millstone moves, and knead it as it is without thinning it with egg white and make its dough neither thick nor thin and then bandage it.
Description of another bandage that binds fractures, dislocations and sprains, take from the wool, cotton,  mallow, resin, licorice, and saffron, ten drachmas each, and mastic and frankincense, five drachmas each, and from wool twenty drachmas, and from the Armenian or Roman clay twenty drachmas. Pound them all together and mix with wool water or egg white if the patient's temperament is hot, then use this bandage because it is a good mixture that binds broken bones quickly and is suitable for most people because of its moderation.
Description of another bandage that is also beneficial for fractures and sprains, take licorice, wool, and white mastic from each ten drachmas, mastic and frankincense from each five drachmas, six drachmas of mallow, twenty drachmas of Armenian clay, pound them all finely, sift them, knead with water or egg white, and use.
A dressing for joints and bones that have moved from their places, and to soothe the pain that comes with them and the fracture of the bone and the broken bone, a soft cloth is taken and dipped in vinegar and cooked oil and placed on the area, and this dressing has no power of setting.
But it is beneficial in soothing hot swelling and driving away pains, especially. A description of another dressing that sets a broken bone, take the dried fig leaf and the poppy leaf, and pound them all together and dress with them wet, a description of another short dressing used when setting a broken bone.
And if you want to analyze the rest of the swelling, take from  the root of saffron and chamomile and violets and ground carnation from each one a part, pound them all together and mix it with plaster if the limb is not rigid, if it is rigid then mix it with watercress water or plain water and use it.
A description of another dressing stronger in analysis than the first one, it is used when a solid swelling occurs with a broken bone, take from the root of saffron and cotton, cumin, crown imperial, marigold, violets, and chamomile from each one a part.
Pound them all together and mix with water of vinegar or fresh water or plaster, all of that according to the heat of the limb and its stillness, as for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book to be placed on the broken limb when it is set.
Except the plasters made from wax and oil alone and described to be medium between thickness and softness, as for Galen, he saw that it should be placed on the broken limb when setting things containing fluff with something of heat like  frankincense, myrrh, and resin and similar to what we mentioned.
A description of a dressing that is beneficial for weakness and pain, take millet, lentils, human hair or bird feathers, saffron, and salt equal parts, pound it and sift it and mix it, and as for the amount that should remain tied .
Then it is necessary that you look, if there was no pain in the patient nor throbbing nor movement of the broken bone from its place do not remove it for many days, but if there occurred at the site severe throbbing or disturbing pain or swelling.
Then hasten and remove it at that time and do not delay that at all and remove the dressing from it, then take a soft cloth or sponge of freedom wet and dip it in lukewarm water and wash with it the site until the throbbing subsides and the pain subsides then leave the limb to rest for an hour.
Then place upon it the soft cloth dipped in vinegar and oil or rose ointment and tie it on it overnight until you safeguard the hot swelling and the swelling of the limb subsides and its pain goes away then return it to gentle tightening and light dressing and do not tighten it as much as the first time.
And attend to it until it heals, if you saw that the swelling and redness and pain and swelling and all the symptoms have gone completely and it needs dressing and tightening then repeat what you did at first whether or not, if nothing of what we mentioned happened in the limb.
Then do not remove it except after three or four or five or seven days and sometimes it may be left for twenty days, all of that according to what appears to you from the state of the limb as we said until when the fracture becomes necessary and near clotting of flesh upon it.
Then also increase at that time in tightening more than your first tightening and also increase in thickening the food of the patient as mentioned before, if you see the site of the fracture has dried up and loosened more than is proper know that the nourishment is prevented from reaching it.
So delay it with lukewarm water at every time you remove it and let that be in every three days and lighten the tightening a little because by this action nourishment flows to the limb and heals quickly, and as for what the ignorant do of those who force to break the limb again.
If one does not first compel what is proper and compels what is crooked, it is an error from their doing and a great evil. Even if it were right, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and practiced it, and I have not seen any trace of that from any of them at all. The Second Chapter.
On a fracture in the skull.
The types of skull fractures are numerous and their shapes are varied, and their causes are diverse. Some fractures are caused by a sword blow and they may be such that the bone is completely severed until it reaches the sac which is beneath the bone. Just as a carpenter does in wood.
Therefore this type of fracture is called "carpenter-like". Or it may be that the sword cut some of the bone and broke its face only, without penetrating to its end. This type of fracture is called "a complete avulsion", and the wound of these two fractures may be either large or small.
And from fractures, some are crushing or bruising, and the cause is a blow with a stone or falling on a stone or something similar. This fracture may be penetrating, having approached the membrane beneath the bone, or it may be in the face of the bone.
And the wound of these two fractures is also either large or small. And from fractures, some are subtle in the bone, like a fine hair's width - this is a slight fissure.
Therefore this type of fracture is called "hair-like". And from fractures, there are some caused by a fall or a stone striking, entering the page of the bone into the inside, making the location concave, as happens to a piece of copper if it is struck, and part of it enters inward.
And this is most common in the soft skull, like that of infants. And all these types of fractures can have fragments that are protruding and non-protruding, and I will come to mention their treatment all together in its place, and we will identify all these types of fracture by examining them.
And its examination with probes and the extraction of rotten flesh from it, and as for the bony kind, it is known that it is revealed on the bone and wiped and smeared on it with charcoal because the fracture appears black, and as for the treatment of the fracture, it is first looked at to the symptoms of the patient.
If you see from his symptoms what clearly indicates fear, such as vomiting bile and stretching and going of the mind and loss of voice and dimness and severe fever and protrusion and redness of the eyes and similar symptoms, do not approach the patient nor treat him because death is upon him with these symptoms in most cases inevitably.
And if you see symptoms that do not frighten you and you hope for his safety, then take hold of his treatment because when the wounded comes to you at first what was wounded and that was in the winter days.
then it is necessary to strive in removing the bone before the fourteenth day on all accounts and if it was in summer days, then it is necessary to hasten in removing the bone before the seventh day before what is under the bone from the membrane rots and exposes those symptoms that we described.
And if the fracture of the bone reached the membrane covering the brain and it was accompanied by crushing and bruising, then it is necessary to cut off the crushed part as I describe to you, which is to shave the head of the wounded patient.
And reveal the bone in any way you are able and according to the shape of the wound and what is hidden from the patient, if a sight comes to you when revealing the bone bleeding or a hot tumor, then respond to that with what is necessary.
Which is to fill the place with a cloth soaked in syrup and rose butter and clean the wound until the tumor subsides and bleeding is prevented, then take hold of straightening the bone and extracting it, which is done in one of two ways of work.
And as for the first face, it is to cut the bone with a delicate incision narrow blade and this picture:
Then you use another incision after this one, slightly different from it, and here is its form: .
Then you use also another incision, which is wider than the second one, and it is necessary for you to have several different incisions, some of them being wider than others and some shorter than others, and they should be very sharp at their edges, and let them be made of Indian steel or good steel.
And use the shank to strike on the incision so that it does not shake the head and hurt it, and if the bone is strong and rigid, then it is necessary for you to drill around it before using the incisions with the holes they call non-immersive holes, but they are called non-immersive holes.
Because they do not exceed the limit of the skull bone beyond it, because the drill has a circular letter on what is below its sharp head resembling a ring or small circle which prevents it from diving and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
And it is necessary for you to take several of these holes, each one suitable for the thickness of that bone, until it prepares for each skull a sharp pointed length and shortness according to the thickness of that skull, and here are three types of images. Of the holes large, medium, and small:.
As for how to drill around the broken bone, it is that you place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has penetrated, then you move the drill to another place and make after each hole a distance equal to the thickness of the reed or so.
Then you cut with the incisions between each two holes and do that with as much gentleness as you can on it, as we said, until you remove the bone either with your hand or with something else from some of the tools you have prepared for it.
Such as the tweezers and delicate forceps, and it is necessary for you to be very careful that the drill or incision does not touch anything from the peritoneum, and if the bone has become strong and healed from the peritoneum, if it was attached to it, then it is necessary for you to remove it.
And smooth out any roughness that remains in the bone with another instrument similar to the chisel, except that it should be more delicate and finer than the rest of the chisels. And if anything of the small bones or splinters remains, take them gently with what you have of instruments.
Then treat the wound after that with the poultices and ointments mentioned, as for the other side of this work it is easy to do and free from difficulty, as Galen mentioned it and praised it highly, as he said. It is necessary for you first to begin by uncovering the part of the bone from the place where the fracture is most severe and prominent until, when you have uncovered that part, you place the tip of this serrated chisel underneath it, and here is its image:.
The serrated part of it should be smooth and not cut anything, and the sharp part of it on its sides going in length as you see so that the serrated part leans against the peritoneum and the face of the sharp chisel is in the bone, then strike the chisel from one side.
With a small mallet until you cut all the bone gently as it rotates and you are safe from the membrane, so there is no fear of any dangerous accident happening to it, even if the surgeon were the most ignorant and cowardly of people, yes, even if he were clumsy.
If anything of the membrane necessary to the bone remains in some places of the bone, scrape it off with the tip of the serrated chisel itself and get rid of it gently for it gets rid of it without harm or fear.
And as for if the fracture of the bone did not penetrate to the membrane and what was severed from the bone was its surface, and there remained roughness and splinters, it is necessary that you remove that roughness and pluck out those splinters with a delicate clamp that has been made of several different sizes so that you can use what suits each place best and most suitable according to what guides you in your work.
