The fractured bone and its shape, and it is necessary that you first apply to the fracture of the bone the broadest of the splints then after that apply a finer one and you will continue to do this until you reach the application of the finest and most delicate of them all.
As for the rest of the small, hairline cracks and the delicate fractures, it is necessary that you apply treatment to each one according to what leads to its healing, which is something not hidden from him who has in this craft even the slightest degree, and look at what we have written and explained in the large fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when you extract the bone, it is necessary that you take a linen cloth equal to the size of the wound and soak it in rose fat and place it on the mouth of the wound then take another folded or tripled cloth. And soak it in syrup and rose fat and place it on the first cloth and do this with the lightest you can so as not to weigh down the peritoneum, then apply a wide bandage from above and do not tighten it except by the amount that only holds the wound.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are assured of the hot swelling, then cover it and after that use the medicines that come with it such as dried iris root, ground cumin, finely chopped licorice, zedoary, and the like, which is to make from these drugs a poultice.
And apply it to the wound as it is dry, and in general, use a medicine that will soothe and not sting, and take care with your effort while treating the wound that it be clean, free of any fat or filth, and do not leave pus to accumulate in it at all.
Because if pus accumulates on the membrane of the brain, it spoils and contaminates it, so that a great affliction befalls the sick person from this, and this can occur in the abdominal cavity when the bone is exposed, especially if it is neglected.
If you look at the surface and see blackness, if it corresponds to the ailments we mentioned, know that he is inevitably going to die. If the blackness came about because of medicine placed upon him and the power of that medicine was to cause this blackness.
Where you ought to take from honey a portion and from rose oil three portions, and beat them well together and smear a cloth with them then place it on the peritoneum and treat him with the types of treatment that are necessary until he is cured.
Chapter Three.
In Setting a Broken Nose.
Know that the nose does not break except its upper parts all or one of them because they are bones, for the lower part of it is cartilaginous and doesn't break but rather suffers from contusion, angulation, and dislocation.
If one of the parts breaks, then it is necessary that you insert your little finger into the nostril and set that fracture from inside with your index and thumb finger from outside until the nose returns to its natural shape. Let this be done by you gently and take care that what you do does not cause pain to the patient.
If the fracture is at the top of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, then it should be set with a blunt instrument that has some thickness, if the fracture is on both sides do the same. And hasten to mend it on the first day of the fracture.
If possible, otherwise after the seventh or tenth day when the hot swelling subsides, then insert a wick of cotton thread into the nostril, if the fracture is on one side, or two wicks if the fracture is on both sides.
Let the wicks be thick to the extent that they fill the nostril, and some of the early orthopedic surgeons said to soak the wicks in butter and change them every day. I don't see that myself, rather it is necessary to moisten the wick with egg white mixed with ground plaster of Paris.
Then the wound is left until the bone is set and the cartilage hardens, and a tube of goose feather wrapped in a soft linen cloth may be introduced into the nostril after the incision to keep the nose fracture rigid and to prevent the patient from being unable to breathe.
And this is not something necessary if you wish, make it and if you wish make the incisions,  if a hot swelling appears in the nose during your work, apply cold to the nose with cotton soaked in vinegar and oil of roses or something from the diachylon ointment.
If a hot swelling does not appear, it is necessary to apply cold from the outside with finely ground semolina and finely ground coriander mixed with egg white, then place a soft compress on top of it and do not tie the nose with anything at all, if the nasal bones are fractured in a small fracture.
Or if they are shattered, it is necessary to cut them and remove them with the instrument that fits them then stitch the incision and treat it with ointments that promote healing and cicatrization, if a wound occurs inside the nose, then it is necessary to treat it with incisions and use lead pipes. Until it heals.
Chapter Four.
In setting a fractured lower jaw.
If the lower jaw is fractured and it is not broken with a wound, if its fracture is only from the outside and it does not break in two and protrude inward, then knowing it is easy.
It is necessary if the fracture is in the right part to insert the index finger of the left hand into the mouth of the patient, and if the fracture is on the left side, then the index finger of the right hand is inserted and it raises the bump of the fracture from inside gently outwards.
And your other hand from the outside of the bone controls it and corrects it, if the jaw fracture has broken in two, then it is necessary to use a splint on both sides for alignment until it can be corrected, if there has been movement in the teeth during the fracture.
Or if it is torn, you should tightly bind it using a thread of gold, silver, or brimstone; then place the splintered part of the bone on top of the broken jawbone, and cover it with folded cloth and lay a large, tight bandage over the cloth, or a piece of leather from a shoe that matches the length of the jawbone. Then you should bind it above as seems appropriate to you, joining it until it doesnâ€™t break and instruct the patient to rest and stay still. And make his food soft foods; if you think something has changed in its shape from any of its sides, then address it on the third day.
Then you should bind it above as seems appropriate to you, joining it until it doesnâ€™t break and instruct the patient to rest and stay still. And make his food soft foods; if you think something has changed in its shape from any of its sides, then address it on the third day. Then you should repair what it has compressed with mill dust mixed with egg whites or semolina flour after removing the splint from it and place a soft pad on top of the bandage; as long as this bandage sticks to it and the bone hasnâ€™t changed, leave it.
Don't remove it until it heals and the fracture strengthens, as this fracture often strengthens in three weeks; if a hot swelling appears during that time, use what we have mentioned repeatedly to soothe it until the swelling goes away.
As for if the fracture is with a wound, and if a piece or pieces of bone have been exposed from it, be gentle in removing those pieces using the tool that seems right to you; if the mouth of the wound is narrow, widen it with a scalpel as much as needed.
Then if you have removed those pieces and nothing is left of them, outline the mouth of the wound if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the ointments that are suitable for it. And suture the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
Treating a Broken Clavicle.
The clavicle most often breaks from the front of the shoulder joint, and its breakage can occur in one of three ways: either it breaks and shatters into two pieces without any splinters, which is easiest to treat.
Now, if splinters occur in the fracture, it is more difficult to set; or if the fracture occurs with a wound, and if the fracture is without a wound, one should summon two assistants, so that one of them holds the arm following the fractured clavicle, and the other stretches the neck toward the other side.
Then, you straighten the fracture with your fingers until it assumes its proper shape and has no projections or irregularity; if more extension is needed, it is necessary to place under the armpit of the injured person a ball of cotton wool or stuffing.
And the bone should be extended according to your need and extend and raise the clavicle, and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is straightened properly; if you cannot draw the end of the clavicle outward because it has gone deep, then the patient should lie on his back.
And a moderate cushion is placed under his knee, and the assistant presses his knee down until the clavicle bone deep rises upwards; then you fix and straighten the fracture with your fingers.
If you feel that a splinter of the clavicle has broken and is moving, it is necessary to cut into it and carefully remove that splinter; if the splinter is trapped in the bone, try to cut it with one of the sections that you have prepared for this purpose. After the clavicle becomes positioned under the tool that preserves the diaphragm, which is a tool made of wood or iron, and this is its image:
It resembles a spoon without irregularity and its width is according to what you need from the size of the bone, and as for its length, it is also according to what you can use for work, and let it have two ends as you see, one end wider and the other narrower.
If the wound you cut when removing the splinter of bone is wide and the hot swelling is controlled, stitch together the lips of the wound; if the wound is slight or you are afraid of the hot swelling, close the wound with cotton wool and lint according to the size of the wound.
If a hot tumor appears, then apply a poultice of crushed rose petal and vinegar or wine to it. As for the tightness of the bone if it is without a wound or incision, it is that you apply a compress made from mill dust mixed with egg white to the bone. And you place the soft splint upon it, then place a ball under his armpit if he needs that, then take a long headscarf whose width is an inch or so, then apply a folded bandage on top of the splint and compress, then take a strip of thin board. Its width is three fingers and its length as well, then insert it into a cloth, then insert that cloth with the splint into the place in the headscarf which falls on the site of the fracture, then tighten the headscarf on the fracture as you turn. And tie it around his neck and under his healthy armpit and under the sick armpit, and turn it several times on each side, and how you see that tightening controls the fracture tightly, which is not hidden from you.
And the essence of all this is that the splint does not leave the broken bone. And therefore it is necessary to check on the patient every day, for whenever the bandage relaxes and you see the splint has shifted, then repair that and tighten the bandage, then make the patient sleep on his back and put a small cushion under his armpit when he sleeps at night.
So that his spine rises with it, and the fracture of the collarbone rises with the elevation of the shoulder or tie his arm to his neck, and do not loosen the bandage unless there is an incident in the area from a boil or tumor for twelve days.
Then renew the compress if you see a reason for it. And tighten in the wrapping and leave it until the fracture of the collarbone heals and consolidates, and it becomes stronger over twenty-eight days. It may be in some people less than that.
Chapter Six.
In the setting of a fracture of the shoulder. Rarely does the shoulder break in its broad place, but rather it breaks from its parts. So whenever it is broken at one place or broken in the middle, this is known by touch, and according to what shape the fracture takes, form [it].
Set it and return it to its natural shape as much as you can with all means, then place on the area flour dust with egg white and linen bandage and put a pad of folded cloth on top of it, then place a wide splint made of wood.
Narrow according to the entire width of the shoulder or slightly wider, and if there is a pus-filled wound under the splint in one place of the shoulders, then set that abscess with linen bandage until the splint lies flat, then pull it tight from above with a long turban.
Pull it tight and make sure the splint doesn't move from its place, and inspect the bandage every day; whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it, and set the splint if it moves from its place. Let the patient lie on his healthy side, and the shoulder should heal in twenty days.
Day or twenty-five days, and if this number is complete, then loosen the bandage and you are safe because it is from bones that do not fear breaking or dislocation, if a piece of bone protrudes and was broken under the skin, cut on it and remove it. And do what was mentioned in a fracture of the collarbone from relieving the hot tumor if anything like that happens.
Chapter Seven.
In the setting of a fracture of the chest.
The chest may break in its middle, rarely is that exposed, but rather its edges are more likely to break and splinter. And from its symptoms if it breaks in the middle, it leans downward and feels severe pain and shortness of breath and coughing.
Perhaps the blood coagulates and a depression becomes apparent in the broken bone, not hidden to the touch. In order to mend it, he should lie on his back with a pillow between his shoulders, then his armpits should be compressed and his ribs gathered together by the hands from both sides, and careful smoothing is done on all sides as much as possible.
And he continues to adjust it until the bone returns to its proper shape, then a bandage and splint are placed on top of it, a plasterboard made from a thin board of cypress or elm and the like is laid over it after being wrapped in cloth. Then he carefully binds it to the broken bone so that it does not shift, and the bandage is passed around in a loop toward the back several times and tightened firmly, then the bandage is checked at all times and tightened whenever it slackens.
And if the necessity arises to remove it due to swelling, pain or a tumor in the place, then immediately remove the bandage and treat whatever appears of that with its appropriate remedy, then return the bandage if you see fit to do so. And keep it tight until it heals.
Chapter Eight.
In mending the ribs if they are broken, know that fractures in them occur in the thick places behind the back and their ends from the front; these are exposed to injury because they are cartilaginous, and knowing this is not hidden to the touch upon examination with the fingers.
And mend them by smoothing the fracture with the fingers on the most suitable side until the shape becomes level as it should be, then bandage and tighten the broken bone with a splint if needed. If the rib fracture is angled inward, it exposes the patient to severe pain.
And a fracture like that which exposes someone with a pneumothorax, for the bone fractures the diaphragm and also exposes him to shortness of breath, cough, and expulsion of much blood; this is difficult to treat, and the ancients have devised many remedies for it.
Some say that the food of the sick should be such as to produce wind and flatulence, so that the abdomen may swell and expand and the fracture be driven outwards. We dislike this lest it become a cause for the occurrence of a hot tumor; if it has not occurred, then if it has occurred, it increases it and confirms it.
Some say that a poultice should be placed on the site and then sucked with force, which is similar to the method except that he fears that the poultice might be drawn excessively to the site because of its weakness, and some say that the site should be covered with wool that has been soaked in hot oil.
And it is made into a cushion between the ribs until it is filled, so that the bandage will be level when wrapped on a turn, then the sick person is treated with the treatment of the shoulder from food and medicine, if the suffering of the patient is severe and unbearable. And the bone used to break through the diaphragm, a painful breaking, and we feared for the sick person, so it is necessary that we make an incision on the site and uncover the broken rib, then we place underneath it the instrument that preserves the peritoneum which its description is given before, and we cut the bone gently and remove it.
Then we gather the edges of the wound if it is large by stitching and treat it with ointments until it heals, if a hot tumor appears during that time, then hasten to put a poultice in rose butter on the site and treat the patient with what soothes the tumor from within also.
And he lies on the side that makes it easier for him to sleep until it heals.
The Ninth Chapter.
On the Treatment of a Sprain of the Back and Neck.
But as for the bones of the neck if they are broken, it rarely happens, and what most frequently happens to them is a sprain, and also the vertebrae of the back; if this happens to someone, and you want to know whether it will heal or not. Look, if you see his hands stretched out and numbed and dead, and he is unable to move them or extend them or grasp them, and if you prick them or pierce them with a needle, he feels nothing and finds no pain in them, then know that he will not heal in most cases, for he is gone.
And if he moves them and feels something when they are pricked or pierced, then know that the spinal cord is sound and the patient will heal with treatment. If a similar condition affects the lower vertebrae, and you want to know whether it will also heal,
Look at his legs. If you see that they are stretched out and the same thing happened to them as happened to the hands, then if he lies on his back, wind and feces come out without will, and if he lies on his stomach, urine comes out without will. And if he lies on his back and wants to urinate, he is unable to do so, then know that he is gone, and do not treat him. If none of this happened to him, the matter was milder, and the treatment for what happened is to try to soothe the hot swelling.
Apply to the affected vertebrae rose oil alone or mixed with roasted egg yolks, apply this three times a day until the hot swelling subsides. Then apply one of the strong, absorbent bandages to the area and bind it tightly with a bandage. Tell him to remain still and in bed.
Let him sleep only on the side that does not cause him pain until he heals. If a piece of bone or something else has broken off from the fracture and healed, it is necessary to make an incision in his skin and remove that bone. Then close the edges of the wound if it is large with sutures, and then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If the last vertebra of the spine, which is the tailbone, has broken, it is necessary to insert your left hand's thumb into the anus.
The broken bone is set with the other hand according to what is possible and fitting, then a bandage and splint are placed on it if needed, and then it is tightened.
If you feel a splinter in it, cut upon it and remove it, and treat the wound with the treatment mentioned before until it heals.
The Tenth Chapter.
In Setting a Broken Hip Bone.
Rare is the fracture of the hip bones, but if they do break, their fracture is that they are shattered at their ends and split lengthwise, and they tilt inwards. This causes pain in the location for the afflicted person as well as a feeling of loss and numbness in the leg that was broken on that side.
And its treatment is to place your hand upon it until you determine the fractureâ€™s form. If the fracture is only at the ends, set that fracture according to what appears fitting to you so that it resembles its natural shape.
If the fracture is lengthwise or if it has tilted inwards, place the afflicted person on his stomach until you are prepared to set that fracture. After setting it, apply a bandage over it, then place a splint of wood or leather upon it and tighten it firmly.
Do not fear for him the shifting of the fracture or removal of the splint.  Set the swelling from the bandaging with what fills it until the tightening takes hold in a straight line, and order the afflicted person to sleep on his back or on his right side, if he is presented with a hot swelling.
So, stop stretching it and setting it until the hot swelling subsides. Apply what soothes it as mentioned before, then return to its setting and tightening as is fitting.  If splinters or fragments from its ends appear in the bone, they should not be removed.
Nor should it be touched but set from outside as we have said, and leave its tightening until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On setting a fracture of the humerus.
The humerus is what is between the elbow to the top of the shoulder. If it breaks, setting it involves one of two ways: take a piece of sturdy wood that is smooth and of medium thickness, in this way:.
And tie at its ends two ropes, then hang it from a high place and the patient sits on a chair, and then throw his broken arm onto the wood until his armpit sticks to the center of the bend of the wood, then hang something heavy from above it.
Or a servant stretches it downwards, then the physician sets the fracture with his hands together until he returns it to what is right, and the other way is that the patient lies on his back and his hand is hung from his neck by a rope.
Then order two servants that one presses above the fracture with his hands, and the other presses below it and each one stretches towards his direction. If you want the stretching to be stronger, then tighten a rope under the fracture and another rope above it, and each one of the servants stretches them towards their directions.
If the fracture is near the shoulder, it is necessary that the [middle] of the rope be under the armpit and the other one under the fracture towards the elbow, and likewise if the fracture is near the elbow, it is necessary that the ropes be placed in that position and on the elbow itself.
Then set the fracture gently without force until when the fracture is even on what is right and united well, then tighten it if there does not appear a hot tumor; if it appears a hot tumor, leave tightening it to the seventh day and put on him a poultice moistened with vinegar and rose grease.
Until when the tumor subsides, then tighten it. The prescription for tightening is that you put the bandage on the fracture, then put a new cloth bandaging over the bandage, and then gather the arm on the humerus itself and place his hand open on his shoulder.
Suspend the sling and support on the arm and shoulder so that the arm acts as a splint if nothing prevents you from doing so, and if nothing has changed in the broken bone. If you are afraid that something might change, then use splints.
It is that you place on the fracture itself a splint which is wider and stronger than all other splints, and let there be between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an increase of three fingers from each side.
Then tighten the splints with the tension mentioned in the beginning of the treatise.
That is, that your tightening on the site of the fracture be tighter.
And as you get farther from the fracture, the tightening should be less. If you see the placement of the splints and the tightening as we have said while you are mending the limb from your hour, then do so.
And if you fear the hot swelling, leave the tightening and splints until the seventh day as we have said, then examine the bandage every three days lest there be a sore or swelling in the place, or the nourishment from reaching the limb due to excessive tightening, so correct all of that according to what we have mentioned.
If you are confident that nothing of that will happen, do not remove the bandage until after many days, and let the patient lie on his back with his hand on his stomach, and a pillow filled with wool be placed under the shoulder moderate.
And examine at every time of night or day lest the shape of the broken limb be disrupted or the bandage relax, so correct all of that with your effort, and make the food of the patient according to the rank we have presented, that it be mild first until when the bone begins to harden.
Then when it is necessary to thicken his food, for it is customary for the arm and leg to harden in forty days, then it is necessary that they be removed and a bath should be used and treated with ointments that are suitable for that, if the fracture is open and suppurating.
Do not remove the bandage for fifty days, or two months.
The twelfth chapter.
In setting a broken arm.
The arm is composed of two bones called the radius and ulna, one small which is next to the thumb and the other large which is situated under the small one from below. Perhaps the larger bone broke alone or the small one alone, and perhaps both broke together.
When the upper small bone broke, its setting is easy and its healing faster. When the lower bone broke, it was a bad fracture and its treatment difficult and slower. If both bones broke together,  if the upper small bone broke....
...then the physician must, when setting it, make his stretching gentle until he straightens it. If the large bone was broken, then he should make his stretching stronger. And if both bones were broken, then he should make the stretching much stronger.
It is necessary to place the hand's shape when setting it and stretching it extended on a cushion, and the thumb of the hand be above all the fingers and the little finger below all the fingers and the patient sitting upright on himself.
And let the cushion be at his height so that the patient is not burdened with difficulty.
Then a servant stretches the arm from below either with his hand or with a bandage, and another servant stretches it from above as well, then the physician sets the bone until he returns it to its best possible shape. If there are splinters in the fracture they should be removed.
Return every splinter to its place with effort. If a protruding splinter appears that penetrates the skin and there is no hope of setting it, then cut it and remove it as described before. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, I have dedicated a chapter to it separately.
Take the treatment from there, and if a swelling appears at first in your suturing, and it is a hot tumor, apply a poultice made of  turmeric mixed with rose oil and white wax. Let it be medium between thick and thin, and tighten the poultice upon it gently until the swelling subsides.
Remove the turmeric poultice and apply a bandage prepared from wheat flour mixed with egg white, then carry out the splints and let the splint placed on the fracture itself be slightly wider and stronger, and know that the number of splints for the arm is usually six.
Whether the fracture was in one forearm or both, then make your tightening on the site of the fracture stronger and tighter, and as much as you tighten upwards or downwards, make the tightening slightly looser as mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
Let the bandage that encases the fracture be a soft and damp one, not too hard, and let the thread used to tighten it be from special cotton, medium between thinness and thickness as we described.  Inspect the limb and the joint after a few days.
If something happens that needs fixing, like an irritation appearing on the limb, it is necessary to bathe the limb in warm water until the irritation subsides, and leave the limb untied for a night until it rests, then resume tightening.
If the tightening has slackened and the bone has moved or something like that, then repair it all to the best of your ability. Also look if, due to excessive tightening, nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb, then loosen it slightly and leave it for days until nourishment reaches it, then tighten it again.
If nothing of what we mentioned has happened to the patient, then he should not be removed until after twenty days or thereabouts. Then hang the patient's arm to his neck and let his arm be moderate, and he should take care from disturbed movements and lie on his back to sleep.
Know that this fracture of the arm mends in thirty days or in thirty-two, and perhaps in twenty-eight days, all of this according to the conditions of humors and states of strength.
The Thirteenth Chapter.
In Setting a Fracture of the Hand and Fingers.
The bones of the palm and the phalanges of the fingers rarely fracture, but they are often bruised. So when a fracture or contusion occurs in the hand...
...it is necessary for the patient to sit upright and before him a stool at an even level.
Then he places his hand on it extended, and the bone-setter stretches the broken bone and sets it straight until when it has come together well, then it is necessary to apply bandages and splints if there is not a hot swelling, then a splint is carried on top according to the place.
And it was put in a soft cloth, if the fracture is down towards the inside of the palm then make a shape like a sphere from cloth and order the patient to grasp it with his broken hand, then tighten it with a long cloth.
And let the splint be from leather which is soft so that it touches the skin with the bending of all the palm, and tighten it as necessary, if the fracture is outwards then it is necessary to make a splint above and another splint below in the palm so that the hand remains open and upright.
Then, the tension is carried as the hand turns and the fingers are tied together with a bandage. If the fracture occurs to one of the phalanges of the fingers, if it is the thumb, then set it correctly and tighten it with the palm, and if you wish, make a small upright splint for him to raise the fracture.
And it does not move, if the fracture occurs to the remaining fingers like the middle finger, the index finger, or the ring finger or the pinky finger, then set it straight and tie it with the following healthy finger or tie them all together on their side, this is better, or place a small upright splint on them.
As we mentioned in the thumb and after inspecting it when you set the bone and after it has moved away from the hot swelling, confront it with what is appropriate if something of that nature occurs as described repeatedly.
The Fourteenth Chapter.
In Setting a Broken Femur.
The femur frequently fractures and is felt because it turns forward and backward, and its treatment consists of tying a band above the fracture and another below it while the patient lies on his face.
Then, each band is extended to its side with moderation, if the fracture is in the middle of the bone. But if it is near the origin of the femur, it is necessary to tie a flexible band, either from wool or something similar.
At the origin of the femur, toward the groin, one should place the band above and the other below the fracture, and similarly if the fracture is near the knee, the band should be near the knee so that the extension goes downwards. Then, the physician smooths the bone with both hands until it returns to the example of the natural shape.
And the bone unites well, and then it is necessary to apply bandages and tightening if no swelling occurs in the limb; but if a hot swelling occurs, leave it for days until the hot swelling subsides, then return to its treatment.
But as for tightening, it is necessary to wind a firm and wide bandage around the fracture two or three times, and leave what remains. Then, wind the leg until the heel reaches the origin of the thigh, and insert a long thread between the femur and the shin near the knee from below, and make the ends of the thread above on both sides.
Then, wind the remaining bandage around the leg and femur, and then place a splint on the femur in the same place as the fracture, and make one splint on the shin bone, and fill the gap between the femur and tibia with a soft linen cloth to equalize the tightening.
Then begin by pulling strongly from the middle at the site of the fracture three or four times. And as you move the band further from the site of the fracture, let your pull be less and softer and more relaxed.
Then proceed to the ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and the leg, bind to it what follows from the splints that are above. Then pass the ends of the thread down until they reach the ankle, and pull on them also to tighten the edges of the splints from the other side.
So that the bandage does not move from its place, then leave the tension on it as long as the limb does not speak of swelling or inflammation or puffiness, and so forth. If something of this happens, proceed to address it and repair what happened of it all, as mentioned before repeatedly.
If there is a fragment of bone that protrudes, it is necessary to smooth it out if you can, otherwise, cut through it and remove it, and treat the wound with what has been mentioned before until it heals. It may be possible to tighten this fracture from the thigh without adding the leg with splints.
As mentioned in the arm and the forearm, except that healing it this way does not expose the patient to lameness, even if it is healed alone without incorporating the leg into it, then there is no doubt that its owner will be lame forever.
And know that the thigh heals in fifty days or a little more or a little less, all of this according to the difference in temperaments and other circumstances.
Chapter Fifteen.
In a fracture of the knee cap.
The kneecap is rarely subject to fracture, but it can be bruised frequently. If a fracture occurs, it is either a split or a fragmentation into parts, and this can occur with or without a wound.
And you rely on that all by touch, and set it by smoothing the separated parts with your fingers until they come together and fit according to what you are able to achieve in terms of accuracy, gentleness, and perfection, then apply a bandage and on top of it place a round splint if you need it.
And you tighten from above the tightening corresponding to it, then you attend to all the circumstances that we described in all fractures like the hot swelling and the like by meeting every occurrence with what is suitable for it until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
In setting a fracture of the leg.
The leg has two bones, one thick and called the femur, and the other thin and called the tibia, and they are subject to the same kinds of fractures as the bones of the arm. Therefore, its setting is like the setting of the arm, and the work is the same. If all the bones are broken, the leg turns in every direction; if the thinner bone is broken, the leg turns forward; and if the thicker bone is broken from below, this is something that is not hidden from you.
So, use the splint and straightening and bind the splints accordingly, except that if a fracture of the leg is an open fracture with many fragments, the splint should be lighter and less forceful, and you should make your effort in setting it easier.
And in the leg, there is something extra to be done compared to the arm, which is that if you have set the splints and finished all your work, take two pieces of pinewood used for smoothing the rooms placed between the cracks of the boards or from palm branches or the like.
And choose from them what is slightly thick and not thin, and let its length be along the leg from the knee to below, then wrap each one with two wraps of cloth along its length, placing one on the leg and the other on the opposite side.
And let it be from the knee to the bottom of the foot, then tie the two splints in three places from the ends and the middle, for by this bridle, the leg is prevented from leaning to the right and left, and a good firmness is achieved, and a wooden gutter may be used along the length of the leg.
And it is placed in it to preserve it from movement and most importantly, this should be done in a fracture if there is a special wound, then the leg is examined every day and great care must be taken of swelling or puffiness or anything else. So when something of that occurs, meet it with what is appropriate until it heals, and the bone of the leg joins in about thirty days.
The seventeenth chapter.
In a fracture of the bones of the leg and fingers.
As for the heel, it does not usually experience a fracture, but as for the bones of the leg, they can experience a fracture, and also the fingers, rarely experience a fracture, but rather injury in most cases.
So if a fracture occurs in the bones of the leg, and you see that some of those bones have overlapped each other, then place the patient's foot on the ground standing upright like walking, then you place your foot on what has risen from those bones and press it down and level it.
Until they return to their places, then carry the bandage and splint from above, and place under the bottom of the foot a small board with two flat tops, then tighten the foot firmly after wrapping it with cloth and all else that you need.
If three or four days pass, then release the bandaging, and you will find the bones level whether they were broken or dislocated, so work as you see, but if some of the fingers are fractured, force them and align them according to what I described for you in setting the fingers of the hand.
Then make for the broken finger a splint along the length of the finger and let it be slightly narrower than it, then place under the foot this board that I described to you and tighten it firmly.  If two or three or more bones are fractured.
Then make on each finger a splint the size of it, wrapped in soft cloth and tighten the board at the bottom of the foot, let each head of the board's two tops be outside the bottom of the foot to secure it well.
It is necessary for you not to forget to take heed of all that we have mentioned to you in all kinds of fractures from the symptoms which we have mentioned and respond to each symptom with what is appropriate.
The eighteenth chapter.
In the fracture of a woman's pubic bone and man's pubic bone.
When a woman's pubic bone is fractured, have her sit upright and then gently bend her back slightly, holding her from behind. Then the midwife should stuff her vagina with cotton until it fills and becomes like a ball in her vagina.
Then shake the woman and raise her hips slightly, so that the cotton comes out until it becomes like a ball at the entrance of the vagina, and the broken bone returns. Then put a pad on her back, and whenever she wants to urinate, remove the cotton gently until she urinates.
And put it back in the way you first inserted it, and then return to her original pad. Do this for seven days or so, because then it will heal. If you want to take a sheep's bladder, tie its mouth with a short pipe and insert the whole bladder into her vagina.
Then inflate the pipe forcefully until the bladder swells inside the vagina, and the fracture returns to its place. Then stuff it with cotton and keep her on what we have described for days until it heals. As for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, this is used to heal it.
And treat it as we have described for the hip bone, and it is not hidden from you that what is right in these strange fractures which rarely occur, especially to those who have some experience and understand my book well because the fracture itself indicates you in most cases.
On the right way to heal and tighten it, so understand. As for when a man's thigh is fractured near his groin, put a phallus into it and then place a bandage of linen on top and bandage it, leaving it for three days or so until it heals.
The Nineteenth Chapter
In the setting of a bone fracture accompanied by a wound.
It is necessary for one who presents with a fracture accompanied by a wound, especially if the bone is large such as the femur or humerus or similar, that you act promptly and separate it from its time if the conditions of trepanation assist you as we have previously mentioned.
If the wound bleeds, it is necessary that you act promptly to cauterize it by applying powdered lead oxide upon it; if nothing else is at hand, then take the fractured bone in that very day and set it, without delay.
If no hot swelling occurs, if a hot swelling does occur, leave its setting until the ninth day until the hot swelling subsides and do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all for it will be exposed to bad conditions.
If the fractured bone is exposed and laid on the skin, it is necessary that you try to return it and straighten it with your own hands gently and slightly; if you cannot return and straighten it with your own hands, return it with this instrument which is made of iron, its length equal to seven or eight fingers. And lay it over the extent of the wound.
And therefore it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of these, according to what he needs for treatment in each kind of fracture, and let them be rounded with a slight thickness so that they will not bend when pressed upon them at the time of working.
And be sharp at the end with a curve at its end and its top to the thickness and from its middle downwards much thinner, and this is its form:
And it is called in Greek "perme" meaning a small awl, so it should be made with its sharp, curved end on the tip of the bone that has been fractured and push it with it once until when the bone returns and becomes some level of straightness then set the ends of the fracture against each other.
If the broken end is delicate and the instrument has not grasped it well, it is necessary to cut off the end of that bone until the instrument can grasp it. If you are unable to set the bone as we have described above at all, then cut it in a way similar to the sections we mentioned.
Or spread it with one of the saws as much as you can, then remove what remains in the bone of roughness and thin shavings. If after setting the bone you find that the patient feels a severe and painful ache, know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
If you are able to set it back in its natural place, do so, for you will benefit the patient greatly. When you have finished setting the bone, dip a cloth in a dark astringent syrup, especially if you are in summer and do not put on the wound any turpentine or anything oily.
Lest it become rotten and corrupted, then use splints while you have time after setting the bone, and leave the wound open by cutting a hole in the bandages with scissors to match the size of the wound.
And be very careful not to tighten the wound with the fracture, for many ignorant physicians have done this and caused their patients either death or a fistula or ulcer. Let your tightening be gentle and slack, unlike the tightening of other fractures.
If the wound is bad or is a large wound and you fear some of the bad symptoms we have described, and it finds pain in its place that is troubling, then it is not necessary to put splints on it. Make for him bandages of stiff cloth in the place of the splints and tighten them with it.
If after a day or two you see that the wound has begun to form pus, remove from it the cloth that you placed on it with the syrup, then use the poultices and ointments that we are accustomed to using for wounds such as the four-part ointment and its like. And it is necessary for you to loosen the bandage.
Inspect the wound every day, in the evening and morning until it closes and heals.  It is necessary to raise the member so that pus drains easily downwards. If many days pass by and the wound does not close or the pus stops flowing, then know that there are small bone fragments within it.
It is necessary to inspect the wound with a probe and if any of those fragments are clean, then remove them and take them out. If any of them were not clean and they were irritating the member and causing pain, then cut them and remove them as much as you can.
If a cold, abscess or another type of corruption and inflammation appears in the wound, then it is necessary to confront each case with its corresponding treatment as described in its chapter.
And of what you should take heed and keep in mind if a large bone breaks and protrudes on the member, such as the femur or humerus, and similar large members, do not attempt to pull it or remove it. For often this can lead to death, so leave it until it decays and perhaps after twenty or thirty days it will fall out on its own.
Then treat the wound if you see a place for treatment, otherwise leave it.
Chapter Twenty
On treating the complication that appears after some fractures.
This complication often appears after the healing of a fracture, especially near joints, making the limb unsightly and perhaps preventing it from performing its natural function.
If the complication is fresh, use the medicines that constrict, such as frankincense, mastic, bitter almonds, rue and quassia, by taking some or all of these and mixing them with a tightening syrup or egg white or vinegar.
And apply it to the complication with a poultice and tighten it firmly, leaving the compression for many days, then release it and reapply another until the complication disappears, God willing, or tighten a sheet of lead over it securely.
For lead has a property that dissolves all that is in the organs, if the complication has hardened and intensified and necessity has driven to its removal, then split it from above and cut away the excess that comes out or strip it with some instruments until it disappears and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In treating a fracture, if it is set and the limb afterwards remains weak and unlike its original nature.
If a bone fracture is set and the limb afterwards remains weak and feeble, that is due to many reasons. One of them is either because of the excessive loosening of ligaments and tying them improperly, or because of overtightening of the ligaments so that nourishment cannot reach the limb, or because of excessive splints, or because of excessive movement at an improper time, or because of low blood in the body of the sick person and his weakness.
And the treatment for this is to nourish the sick person and enrich his body until blood increases in him, and using hot baths and bringing him joy and happiness, and so on, then  apply a poultice to the limb to attract much nourishment to it or continue bandaging it with lukewarm water. Until nourishment flows in it and it returns to its natural shape.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
In treating a fractured bone if it is set crooked and prevented from functioning properly.
If, after healing, a limb shows a malformation or protrusion of the fractured bone or a complication that obscures its appearance. Unless the limb is prevented from its natural function, it is not right to accept the saying of one who claims to break a limb from the top, for many ignorant physicians and bone-setters used to do this in our country.
This act is highly condemned and leads to great harm, even if the malformation and complication are pliable, it should be treated with water that has been boiled with herbs such as leaves of thyme, its root, crown imperial, and similar ones.
It is bandaged with a plaster of a relaxing consistency, such as the well-made diachylon plasters, or take saliva from the root of the plant called "al-khatim," and beat it with chicken fat and sheep tallow and bandage with it, or take the fleshy fig and pound it with pigeon dung and similar medicines that are called anti-scarring.
And it is treated with a continuous, fine massage using the hands and the use of movement to all directions at all times.  If the curvature has come forward and intensified and become hardened and necessity requires its treatment with iron.
Where it is necessary to cut above, release the connection of the bone and cut what remains of the curvature or bone with cuts of gentleness and use gentleness and care in that, then treat the wound as mentioned before until it heals.
Chapter twenty-three.
The jaw is an exit of a joint from its place, hindering movement and disfiguring the limb, causing severe pain for the patient.  So when it happens to anyone, one should immediately attend to putting it back in place.
One should not delay it at all because if one delays, the swelling of the place and pus will make it difficult to put the jaw back in place. Therefore, it is not permissible to delay or move or stretch while it is swollen because it often causes spasms and painful aches for the patient.
But if it happens, one should immediately attend to treating the patient and then let it rest until the swelling subsides a little, then warm the limb with warm water and oil, and then gently put it back in place and treat each part as mentioned previously in its place.
And I have arranged the chapters of the jaw also according to what was mentioned before in the fracture, from the top of the body to the bottom,.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On treating the lower jaw fracture.
The jawbones rarely dislocate except in a few cases, and their dislocation happens on one of two sides: either they deviate slightly from their places, relaxing a little, or they completely dislocate entirely, so that they relax. Towards the chest until the patient's saliva flows and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his jaw, and he is unable to speak properly with his tongue, but if it were a slight dislocation, then in most cases it returns to its place by itself easily.
But if the dislocation were complete, then it is necessary to hasten its return quickly and not delay it at all, which is that a servant grasps the head of the patient and the physician puts his thumb into the root of the jaw inside his mouth, if the jawbone were from one side.
Or puts both thumbs in if the jawbone were from both sides, and the rest of his fingers from outside he smooths them, and orders the patient to relax his jaw and release it to go in all directions, and the physician smooths the jawbone and pushes it until it returns to its place.
If it is difficult to return, especially if both jawbones were all dislocated, then apply a compress with hot water and oil until it becomes easier to return them, and do not delay their return at all as we have said, so if they return and settle, and the patient's mouth closes and does not relax, then in that case, place on them padding of linen with compresses made from wax and oil.
Then it is gently tied with a loose bandage, and the patient's sleep is on his back, and his head is cushioned between two pillows so that he does not move it right or left, nor does he make an effort to chew anything but makes his food soft.
Even if the pain has gone and the jaw has knitted, let him eat what appears to him, and let him use it gently and not force his mouth open when eating, drinking, and yawning until the jaw has knit and healed.
If it is difficult to bring back the jaws if they have been dislocated for a while and have not returned to their places, then there are often inflammations and persistent headaches and perhaps the abdomen of the patient swells and perhaps he vomits repeatedly without reason. If you see this, know that he has become corrupted.
And many die from this affliction within ten days.
The Twenty-Fifth Chapter.
On Setting a Broken Collarbone and Shoulder
As for the collarbone, it does not dislocate from the inside due to its connection with the chest; however, it can dislocate outwards and this is evident to the touch. To set it, have the patient lie on his back and extend his arms.
Then press the position with your hand, pressing forcefully, and it will return. Then place a bandage and padding on it and tighten it. As for its end that is connected to the shoulder, it rarely dislocates; however, if it does dislocate one day, it should be returned and set as mentioned above, and whatever seems appropriate to you.
Then place a bandage, padding, and tightening on it, and order the patient to remain at rest and quiet until he heals. With this very treatment, the end of the shoulder is returned if it also moves from its place.
The Twenty-Sixth Chapter.
On Setting a Dislocated Shoulder
Know that the shoulder dislocates in three directions: one is that it dislocates towards the armpit downwards, the second is that it dislocates towards the chest, and perhaps it dislocates above the shoulder, which is rare.
It does not depart to the rear of the shoulder's place nor to the front of the nerve's place, and most of all it departs and exits downwards towards the armpit, especially in those whose flesh is little because it exits in them quickly and enters quickly.
But as for those whose flesh is much, it is the opposite, I mean that it exits with difficulty and enters with difficulty, and perhaps a blow or fall occurs to some people so the shoulder joint swells and there is hot phlegm in it, and they think it has dislocated, so it is necessary to examine it until you ascertain its health.
Then you progress in its treatment, and the dislocation is recognized if it is downwards towards the armpit, if you advance with the treatment of the dislocated shoulder joint and the healthy one, then you find a visible difference between them, and you find the head of the shoulder in it confused, and under the armpit upon touch, the head of the shoulder as if it were an egg.
And the sick person is not able to raise his hand to his ear nor to move it in all movements, and likewise if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards then you find that apparent to the touch, it is not hidden.
And this dislocation may be easy to return if it was fresh or the sick person is a child, and its return that a servant raises his hand upwards then you place your thumbs under his armpit and raise the joint with force upwards to its place and the servant raises his hand and stretches it upwards.
Then he lowers it downwards then it returns quickly, if it does not return with what was mentioned and the dislocation has been for many days, then it is necessary for the sick person to bathe in hot water and use the poultice which relaxes and softens like cooking the root of marshmallow and cumin and crown imperial in water and use.
Then the sick person lies on his back and you place under his armpit a ball of wool that is moderate between soft and hard, then the doctor puts his heels on the ball and grasps the hand of the sick person with his hand and extends it downwards.
He pushes the ball with his leg since it returns to its place, and if you wish, return it in this manner: You bring a man taller than the sick one and make him stand from the side of the flank, and insert his knee under the armpit of the sick person and raise his armpit up until the sick one is suspended in the air. And another servant pulls the hand of the sick person down to his belly, and if the sick person is light, it is necessary to hang something else on him to weigh him down since it returns the jaw in an hour, and it can also be returned in another way.
And this is by placing in the earth a long plank whose head is rounded like the pestle of a mortar, neither thick nor thin, and then it is placed under the armpit of the sick person after putting on the head of the plank a flexible rope, and the sick person standing on the length of the plank.
Then he stretches his hand down on the other side and also stretches his body from the other side with force, since the joint returns to its place quickly, if it is difficult to return it by all that has been mentioned, then use this treatment which is to take a plank about two arms long. And its width the size of four fingers and its thickness the size of two fingers, it has a rounded head to make it easy to enter into the depth of the armpit in this form:.
Then a flexible rope is tied on the rounded head so that the wood does not harm the sick person, and then it is placed under the armpit, and the whole hand and arm are stretched on the plank downwards, and the plank is tied to the shoulder, forearm, and hand.
Then the arm is placed on a beam.  The stretcher is held across and the hand is stretched down, and the rest of the body is left suspended from the other side since the joint enters in an hour, so if it enters in any way that is possible, then it is necessary to place under the armpit a ball of moderate size made of wool.
Then, he applies the prepared dressing of powdered millstones mixed with mastic and egg white to the whole shoulder as it turns over, then draws the ball tightly from under the armpit and wraps it onto the dressing above with a bandage, and ties his hand up to his neck.
And he leaves him without moving his hand for seven days, and it is necessary that he make the sick person's food little until the limb grows strong, then after the seven days or five, he trains with movement, if it has held firm and not relaxed, then he is healed.
If the joint dislocates repeatedly due to moisture or another cause, then it is necessary to use a three-layered bandage on it as mentioned previously in the chapter on bandages,  and if all of this was done and the bandage was removed after seven days and the joint did not hold firm.
The dressing was reapplied and tightened several times but it did not hold firm, and it fell and relaxed, and she could not raise it above, know then that the nerve in the head of the shoulder has been severed or stretched or relaxed, know then that the joint will never hold firm in its place.
As for the jaw that is near the chest and breast, and behind its side, it is treated by pushing and stretching with the hands until it returns, and all bandages and treatment are used on it until it heals, if it presents after healing a numbness in the limb and slows down in movement.
So the sick person should use the bath repeatedly until that numbness softens and returns to its original nature.
Chapter Twenty-Seventh.
In the Treatment of Shoulder Dislocation.
The shoulder joint dislocates with difficulty, and also returns with difficulty. It dislocates in all directions, especially forward or backward. Its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is located under sight and touch, to any shape it has dislocated.
If you set the dislocated elbow against the sound one, it will be clear to you by a visible appearance of dislocation at the joint and he will not be able to bend his arm or touch his fingers, and it is necessary that you hasten and put back the broken bone from its place before a hot swelling appears.
For if a hot swelling appears to it, its return is difficult and perhaps it will not heal at all, especially if the fracture is to the back, for it is the most difficult of all kinds of fractures and the most painful, and often death comes with it.
And to set it if it is of what can be returned, he should extend his servant's hand with both hands and his arm outstretched and the physician's hand above the elbow and below him while he pushes the joint with his thumbs or the root of his palms until it returns to its place.
And as for if the fracture is to the front, it may be returned by bending the hand once until it strikes with the root of its palm the elbow that adjoins it, and if the fracture does not yield to return, then employ strong and very powerful stretching which is that he extends the arm with two servants.
And he shall hold the patient with two servants also that it may not move during stretching, and then the arm is rotated in every direction after having been wrapped on his hand with a folded cloth or a wide sheet, and when the physician begins with his hands the joint, he shall rub it with oil so that it aids in the easy slipping of the joint.
Then he shall push the joint with great force until it returns, and after its return, it is necessary that a bandage containing compression and drying with egg white be placed on it and tightened firmly and the arm should be suspended from the neck of the patient and left for several days.
Then it is removed if the joint has settled in its place, so remove the bandage and leave it, but if you see that the joint did not become firmly set, then reapply the bandage and splint and leave it for several days until it becomes firm, then remove it, but if a bruise happened to him after the return of the joint and its removal.
If movement is difficult, he should apply moisture in the bath and gentle massage and stroking until it softens, or place on the joint a sheep fat pad then tie it and leave it on him for a day and night then remove it and put him in the bath, if he sweats, massage the joint with moderate friction.
Then put the pad back on him a second and third time with entering the bath until it softens, and if you wish to place on him wet cow dung lightly mixed with butter and tighten it, do that several times for it will soften and return to its original state.
The Twenty-Eighth Chapter.
In the Treatment of Fracture of the Radius.
The wrist of the hand is often fractured and its fracture is easy to set compared to other joints, except that it is necessary to quickly set the fracture at the time it occurs before a swelling or hot inflammation arises in the place.
To set the fracture, place the limb's wrist on a board and stretch the servant's hand, then the physician puts his palm on the joint's prominence and pushes it until it returns, except that it is necessary to look if the fracture has shifted to the inside of the hand.
So place the patient's palm facing up on the board when stretching and setting, but if the fracture is protruding to the outside of the hand, let his palm be placed down on the board so that the physician's hand falls on the same prominence of the joint; if it returns from then on, otherwise tighten it with a bandage that relieves swelling.
And leave him, do not bother him, for he cannot endure it and you will not be able to set it again after days have passed except that the joint remains on its side and does not harm the patient except if the hand is stretched and cannot grasp anything.
Then you will know that the nerve is severed or pinched, there is no remedy except to tighten it with a poultice, perhaps it will benefit and perhaps it will not benefit at all. If the radius is set, apply the bandage we have described then tighten it and leave it for five days.
Then he should soften it and exercise the hand, if its movement is hindered and something from the body is present in it, he should soften it with warm water and rub it repeatedly until it softens.
The twenty-ninth chapter.
On the treatment of fractured fingers.
Fingers may fracture in every direction, and when a finger is fractured to the outside or inside of the palm, extend the finger and push the fracture with your thumb until it returns. Then tie the head of the finger and suspend it toward the direction it was fractured.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it stands straight upright. On that day, if it is at night, tie it in the same manner, but during the day loosen it and exercise it by moving it and tie it up at night. Do this for several days until it becomes strong.
And so do the same if it is fractured to the inside of the hand, tie it toward the same direction and do what you did initially until it heals. Do the same for all other directions of fracture.
The thirtieth chapter.
On the treatment of a fractured vertebra.
When a fracture occurs in one of the vertebrae of the back or neck, if it is a complete fracture or many vertebrae have shifted from their places, there is no treatment because death quickly comes to the patient.
A sign of this is that the patient's feces come out involuntarily, he cannot hold it and many times some of his limbs relax either his legs or arms or one of them. As for if one vertebra shifts from its place, it often continues to shift.
And it shifts in four directions, the one that shifts to the back is called a hump, as for its treatment, look, if the hump occurred from childhood, there is no treatment for it, nor any cure at all. But as for the one that occurred due to a fall or blow or something similar.
The ancients mentioned it with many types of treatment in lengthy discourse, most of which is no longer beneficial. I have summarized what is sufficient from that, making little known about much of what they brought because I have approximated the meaning and explained it, and illustrated the instrument differently than they described and explained it.
I say that the hump that occurs in front of the chest has no remedy or cure for it, nor does the one on both sides.  But what is treated from those is the one that occurs in the back, especially as I describe it, which is to stretch the patient on his face.
On a flat workbench near a wall and spread under it a damp mat so as not to harm his chest, then place a standing board embedded in a pit in the earth at one end of the bench towards his head and another board towards his feet at the other end of the bench.
And a servant holds the board and let it not be firmly fixed in the pit, and another servant holds the other one in that position, then wrap around the patient's chest and under his armpit with a soft, tight bandage and stretch the end of the bandage to the board at his head and tie it there.
Then tighten with another bandage above his hip and above his knee and at his ankle, then gather all the bandages together and tie them to the other board at his feet, and each servant extends the board towards his side with the bandage, and the two boards do not move from their fixed positions.
Unless they are not firmly fixed as we said, and the physician places his hands on the hump with force until it returns, or he puts a board on it and leans on the board with his legs until it returns, if it does not return by this treatment, then take a board that is about three cubits long.
And dig in the wall that we said should be near the patient a place where the end of the board can enter, then place the middle of the board on the hump and the physician places his legs on the other end and pulls tightly until it compresses the hump.
And return it to its place, and if you wish to make it with the lever that is operated by hand, which is to insert into the earth at the head of the patient in the end of the bed two pieces of wood, each one being an arm's length long, and at his feet in the end of the bed also two pieces of wood.
And let there be after each piece of wood a span, and it has been made in each piece of wood a hole through which the lever runs, and tie the wood all to the earth securely so that it does not move even a bit, and insert a round rod which is the lever that twists the bandage in the holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its end a hole has been tied with a stick, one span long that twists, and in the other two pieces of wood similar to that, then tighten the bandage that you tightened on the chest of the patient in the lever that is at his head, and the bandages that you tightened on his leg in the lever that is at his feet.
Then a servant stands by each lever, operating his hand with the instrument that twists the lever, and the physician smooths the hump as we have mentioned before, and this is the image of the lever, the bed, and the patient:
Then after the wound returns and the place is level, it becomes necessary to carry the dried bandage with egg white then the plaster, and then place on top of the bandage a board splint whose width is three fingers or so, and its length is as much as the hump takes.
And on some beads that are true, then tie it with the bandage that is necessary and the patient uses gentle food until he heals, if some of the protrusion remains in the place at the end of the healing, then it becomes necessary to use medicines that soften and relax along with the use of the board that we described before for a long time.
And a lead plate may be used for that, and it may appear that there is a protrusion at the end of the last vertebrae in the back, and it is thought to be loosening, and this may be an extra bone that has protruded so do not expose it to this treatment, because perhaps death will occur.
Chapter Thirty-One
In the Treatment of a Dislocated Hip.
Know that the hip joint and the elbow joint are only subject to dislocation and not, like other joints, to slight displacement or subluxation. And the hip joint dislocates in four directions.
That is, it dislocates inwards, outwards, forwards, and backwards, and most commonly it dislocates inwards, and least commonly it dislocates forwards or backwards.  And the sign of its dislocation inwards is that if you compare the leg of the healthy limb to the sick one, the latter will be shorter.
And the knee is more prominent than that of the healthy one, and the sick person is unable to bend their leg at the hip joint, and the place beneath the hip joint appears soft and malleable because the head of the femur has gone there.
And the sign of dislocation outwards is that its symptoms are opposite these, and the sign of dislocation forwards is that he extends his leg fully except that he cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if he tries to walk forward he cannot do so.
And he is obstructed in urination and his hip bone is warm, and when he walks his weight is on his heel, and the sign of dislocation backwards is that he cannot extend the knee and he cannot bend it before bending the hip joint, and his leg is also shorter than the other one.
And the hip joint is relaxed and the head of the femur is at the place of the lower back, but as for the treatment of these types of dislocation, if you look, if the dislocation is old and has been with its owner for a long time and he did not try to treat it and it remained in its state then there is no treatment at all.
So it should not be exposed to treatment, but as for the one who has dislocated it recently and from any of the four directions of dislocation, hasten to twist the joint and extend it inwards and outwards and move it right and left so that perhaps.
If he returns in this way and does not need any other treatment, if he does not return like this then it is necessary to prepare a servant with strong hands who will stretch his leg from below either by his own hands or by a bandage tied on his leg above the knee and another servant who will stretch him from above.
That he should insert his hands under his armpit and then tighten with a soft bandage on the origin of the thigh, and another servant grasps the end of the bandage. A third will stretch, either from the front from the side of the shoulder blade to the collarbone or from behind to the back of the spine.
And they will all stretch at once until the patient is raised with his body from the ground and remains suspended. This type of stretching is a common type for the four types, if the jaw returns as we mentioned, otherwise it is necessary for each type of what I mentioned from special treatment.
As for its special return if the jaw is inside, it is necessary that the patient lie on his right side and then a bandage is placed on the origin of the thigh between the head of the femur and the place below the shoulder blade.
Then the bandage is stretched upwards from the side of the shoulder blade to the top of the body towards the collarbone, and then another strong servant takes hold with his arms around the thick part of the thigh of the afflicted person and stretches outwards with a strong stretch, for it returns to its place.
And this type is easier than all the other types of treatment that bring back this member, if it becomes difficult for you and he does not respond to entering this type of treatment at all, then it is necessary to tie both legs of the patient together with a strong, soft bandage on the heels and knees.
And after each one of them, it should be the width of four fingers, and the afflicted leg should be stretched more than the other by the length of a finger, then the patient is hung from his head on a piece of wood that is in the house and its distance from the ground is the length of an arm.
Then you order a strong boy to embrace the head of the thigh and another boy clings to the patient, and the other boy who is embracing the thigh pushes him strongly so that the joint returns to its place quickly, as for its particular return if the bone is outside.
It is necessary for the patient to lie on his back as we described for the one with a hump, and tighten the bandage on his affected leg especially and on his chest, then place one plank at his feet and the other at his head, then place an extra plank in the middle of the bed firmly secured.
A wet cloth is wrapped around it so as not to harm the patient, making the plank between his thighs so that he does not pull down during extension, then each servant stretches towards his side and the doctor adjusts the hip with his hands, if it responds to returning.
And if not, place the board on him and press it as we mentioned in the hump except that it is necessary for the patient to lie on his healthy side, and if the dislocation is forward, it is necessary to stretch the leg of the patient once while he is on this description exactly on the bed.
And the doctor places the palm of his right hand on the affected rib and presses it with his other hand, and at the same time he makes the pressure extended downwards towards the knee, and if the dislocation is backward, it is not necessary to stretch the patient downwards.
And it is raised on the ground, but it is necessary for him to be placed on a solid thing as it is also necessary to be from his hip and knee outside according to what we mentioned of his lying on the bed on his face and bandages tightened.
As we said earlier, it is necessary to use the clamp with the board also on the place to which the joint exited, and if the return of the hip joint is complete as it should be and its sign of returning is not hidden from you, which is that you extend the leg of the patient.
If you see them both level and the patient contracts and extends his leg without difficulty, know that the limb has returned to its proper place. Then bring together the thighs and apply a bandage and tighten it with a turban tightly so that the hip does not move in any direction.
And the patient must remain still for three or four days, then release the bandage and compare the legs. If you see them equal in length, know that the fracture has set, so release the patient to walk. But if you see any slackness in it,
return and bandage it and tighten it as you did at first, and leave him also for three days, then release it and he should walk slowly on it for a few days until it strengthens, if God wills.
The Second Thirty-Second Chapter.
In the Treatment of Fractures of the Knee.
The knee breaks in three directions: inwards, outwards, and downwards (meaning to the rear), and it does not break forward from the patella. The sign of a fracture is that you order the patient to press his leg against his thigh.
If it does not stick to the thigh, know that the knee is fractured. To set all sides of a fracture, have the patient sit with his legs extended if he is able, and have an assistant behind him to hold his waist and gently pull him back slightly. Then you sit on his thighs.
and press your back against his face and place his leg between your legs, then grasp his knee with your hands and intertwine them between your fingers on his knee. Then compress the sides of his knee firmly with your hands, and have another assistant extend his leg until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign that it has returned is that you press the leg to the thigh in a gentle way, not forced. Then bandage it and stick the leg to the thigh and then tie them together with a strip for three or four days, then release them, and let him use only little walking for a few days until it strengthens.
If it is difficult for you to return it as we have described, then use strong traction with the ligatures which I have previously described in the treatment of the hip until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Third.
In the Treatment of Broken Heel.
The heel may be dislocated slightly, or it may be completely fractured. Its fracture may be inward or outward. The sign of its fracture is that the heel appears swollen and protruding in the direction to which it has fractured.
As for the treatment of its dislocation, it is easy to return it by gently stretching it with the hands until it returns. But as for its treatment if it has been completely fractured, it is necessary that the patient sit and a strong servant grasps him from behind his back in the middle.
Then you grasp his foot with your right hand from its upper part and with your left hand from below his foot in the position of the ankle, then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, then with your left two times, then stretch it with your left and push the front of the foot with your right toward the leg without force.
Do this twice as we have described, then push the front of the foot toward the leg in the third time while you pull on the ankle. If it returns in one or two times in this manner and you see the foot level, otherwise repeat the operation on it for it will return.
If it does not return for you as we have described, then lay the patient on his back on the ground and insert and wedge into the earth firmly so that it lies between his thighs, and having wrapped a cloth around it so that it does not harm the patient, then let a servant hold his thigh.
Then let another servant stretch the leg either with his hands or with a bandage tying it on the neck of the leg, then each servant stretches in the opposite direction to that of his companion and the wedge standing between the thighs of the patient holds it so that his body does not be pulled down when stretching, then the physician adjusts the fracture with his hands.
And another attendant holds the healthy leg down so that the jaw returns quickly, and when the jaw has returned and you have seen its correct return, take the bandage and splint and tighten it with strong ties, and secure the foot to the foot by a bandage, and it is necessary to keep away from the nerve which is above the heel from behind.
So that the bandage does not become too tight and hurt him, then leave it for two or three days; if the bandage relaxes, tighten it again, then release it on the third or fourth day, and the patient refrains from walking for forty days.
If he wishes to walk before this period, it is not safe that the jaw may break and be spoiled, and after that it will not accept treatment; if a hot swelling appears on him, then it is necessary to use what we have described elsewhere in the treatment and soothing until it goes away.
Chapter Forty-Third.
In the Treatment of a Broken Finger.
It is necessary to straighten what has broken from it with a gentle stretching, which is not difficult but easy; and if the fracture is in some of the bones of the heel, then it is necessary to make the patient's foot on a flat surface of the ground.
Or on a board while standing like a walker, then you place your foot on what is protruding from those joints and press it with your foot forcefully until it returns and you see that it has become straight and there is no protrusion in the place.
Then place under the sole of his foot a board that takes the whole foot, which has two ends, then tighten it with a strong and tight tightening for three days, then release it and protect him from walking for many days until it strengthens and is safe from returning, God willing.
Chapter Fifty-Third.
In the Kinds of Fracture That Occur With a Wound or with a Break or with Both Together.
When something of that sort happens and its treatment and healing are delayed, it often leads to death.
Therefore, it should not be treated by anyone other than someone skilled in the craft, with long experience, gentle, compassionate, thoughtful, not hasty or reckless, and he should use at the beginning only medicines that subdue hot tumors and leave the sick to their rest.
To the Almighty, I only ask for well-being from illness with slight suffering, and since some hope appears in it to you, return him to his health at first before the hot tumor develops.
If the limb returns as I desired, use the method that subdues hot tumors and treat the wound with ointments suitable for drying it. If the fracture is accompanied by bone splinters breaking out, then remove them. And follow in this what we mentioned earlier regarding simple diseases in their respective places.
And take care in your efforts and purify yourself from entering the path of recklessness as I have previously advised you, for that is more beneficial for your sake and safer for your presence, God willing.
